(1){{Infobox NFL player}} Abner Haynes (born September 19, 1937 in Denton, Texas) is a former college and professional football player in the United States. 
 (2)Haynes is a graduate of North Texas State University (now the University of North Texas) where Abner Haynes and Abner Haynes then teammate Leon King integrated college football in the state of Texas in 1956.[1] 
(3) In 1960 Abner Haynes chose to play for the American Football League's Dallas Texans and led the league in rushing attempts, yards, and TDs in its first year. 
(4) Haynes helped launch the AFL in 1960, when Abner Haynes was the fledgling league's first Player of the Year, and its first Rookie of the Year. 
(5) Abner Haynes captured the AFL's first rushing crown with 875 yards, and also led the Texans in receiving, punt returns, and kickoff returns. 
(6) Haynes spent three years in Dallas and two with same franchise when it became the Kansas City Chiefs. 
(7) The Kansas City Chiefs, and the North Texas Eagles will later retire Abner Haynes number (28) Jersey in honor of Abner Haynes many achievements. 
 (8)Haynes still owns 10 franchise records, including most points in a game (30), most touchdowns in a game (5), and most career combined yards (8,442). 
(9) Over Abner Haynes career Abner Haynes was regularly among the American Football League's top ten rushers, ranking third all-time, and the all-time leader in touchdowns, with 46. 
(10) Abner Haynes was Hall of Fame head coach Hank Stram's most versatile and dangerous weapon from 1960–62, amassing 43 touchdowns and 4,472 yards on rushes and receptions. 
(11) In 1962, Abner Haynes helped the Texans win the AFL Championship in the classic double-overtime victory over the two-time defending champion Houston Oilers. 
(12) At the time it was the longest professional football championship game ever played. 
(13) In that game, Haynes scored touchdowns on a 28-yard pass reception from quarterback Len Dawson, and on a 2-yard run. 
 (14) "He was a franchise player before they talked about franchise players," praised Stram. 
(15) "He did it all -- rushing, receiving, kickoff returns, punt returns. 
(16) Abner Haynes gave us the dimension we needed to be a good team in Dallas." 
 (17)The 6-foot-1, 200 pound (91�kg) Haynes, who had great speed and dazzling moves in the open field, set AFL records with 5 touchdowns in a game and 19 touchdowns in a season in 1961, and with 46 career rushing touchdowns. 
(18) Abner Haynes also played for the Denver Broncos, the Miami Dolphins, and the New York Jets. 
 (19)During Abner Haynes 8 professional seasons, Haynes carried the ball 1,036 times for 4,630 yards, a 4.5 average; caught 287 passes for 3,535 yards, a 12.3 average, and 20 touchdowns; returned 85 punts for 875 yards, a 10.3 average, and 1 touchdown; and ran back 121 kickoffs for 3,025 yards, a 25.0 average, and 1 touchdown. 
(20) Abner Haynes 12,065 combined yards is the American Football League record. 
(21) Haynes had three games in which Abner Haynes gained 100 or more yards on 14 or fewer carries, and was selected to the All-Time All-AFL second team. 
(22) Abner Haynes has a program called Heroes of Football which connects former professional players with their communities. 
(23) Haynes is the cousin of Sly Stone, Rose Stone, and, Feddie Stone of Sly and the Family Stone.

(1){{Infobox musical artist}} (1)Adrienne Eliza Bailon (born October 24, 1983) is an American actress, singer-songwriter, dancer, and television personality. 
(2) Adrienne Bailon first rose to prominence as a member of the all female group 3LW, along with Kiely Williams and Naturi Naughton. 
(3) The trio released two studio albums together; their 2000 self titled debut album and the 2002 album A Girl Can Mack. 
(4) Following Naughton's departure from the group, Williams and Bailon released their third studio album, Naughty or Nice at the end of 2002. 
(5) In 2005, their first greatest hits album, Neva Get Enuf was released, and the following year they released the lead single from their upcoming fourth album, Point of No Return, featuring new member Jessica Benson. 
(6) The album was never released, though it did leak online. 
(7) In 2007, the group officially split up. 

(8)Bailon and Williams worked together in the Disney channel musical The Cheetah Girls, about four teenage girls who form a girl group. 
(9) The film's soundtrack peaked at number 33 on the Billboard 200, and has been certified 2x Platinum by the RIAA. 
(10) The original four girl lineup went on to star in the film's sequel, released in 2006. 
(11) The film's soundtrack was their first to enter the Top 10 of the Billboard 200, and has since received a platinum certification from the RIAA. 
(12) Following the departure of Raven Symone from the group, they released their first Christmas album, Cheetah-licious Christmas, and their official debut album, TCG. 
(13) Bailon and the other remaining members of the group went on to star in the 2008 film The Cheetah Girls: One World, which was filmed in India. 
(14) The film's soundtrack peaked inside the Top 20 of the Billboard 200, and has sold an estimated 300,000 copies in the US alone. 
(15) The group officially disbanded in December of 2008 to pursue solo projects. 
 
(16)Aside from Adrienne Bailon work in 3LW and The Cheetah Girls, Bailon has went on to have Adrienne Bailon own acting career. 
(17) In 2005, Adrienne Bailon appeared in the film Coach Carter portraying the role of Dominique. 
(18) Adrienne Bailon has appeared in the made-for-television films Taylor Made{{dn}} and Buffalo Dreams, both released in 2005 as well. 
(19) Bailon had a lead role in the made-for-television film All You've Got, alongside R&B singer Ciara. 
(20) The film debuted on MTV in 2006. 
(21) Bailon also portrayed the lead role of Erma in the 2006 comedy film Cuttin' da Mustard, alongside actor Wesley Jonathan. 
(22) Bailon is also set to star in the 2012 film I'm In Love With a Church Girl. 
(23) Aside from Adrienne Bailon work in film, Adrienne Bailon has also guest starred in numerous television series. 
(24) Adrienne Bailon has appeared in the Disney series That's So Raven as Alana Rivera and The Suite Life of Zack & Cody, along with the other members of The Cheetah Girls. 
(25) Bailon was in a relationship with Rob Kardashian from 2007 to 2009, and made several guest appearances on both Keeping Up with the Kardashians and Kourtney and Kim Take New York. 
(26) Bailon has also done solo music work, contributing to the Confessions of a Shopaholic soundtrack, and numerous guest appearances. 
(27) Several of Adrienne Bailon solo songs have leaked online, and Bailon has confirmed that Adrienne Bailon debut album is slated for a 2013 release date. 
(1){{Infobox scientist}} (1)Agostino Bassi, sometimes called de Lodi (25 September 1773, near Lodi, Lombardy – 8 February 1856, in Lodi), was an Italian entomologist. 
(2) Agostino Bassi preceded Louis Pasteur in the discovery that microorganisms can be the cause of disease (the germ theory of disease). 
(3) Agostino Bassi discovered that the muscardine disease of silkworms was caused by a living, very small, parasitic organism, a fungus that would be named eventually Beauveria bassiana in Agostino Bassi honor. 
(4) In 1844, Agostino Bassi stated the idea that not only animal (insect), but also human diseases are caused by other living microorganisms; for example, measles, syphilis, and the plague. 
 
(5) Agostino Bassi was the son of a wealthy farmer and a lawyer who also had a passion for biology. 
(6) However, Agostino Bassi father did not want him to take up biology, but wanted him instead to look after the family's property, to become a civil servant and to join the Imperial administration. 
 
(7)Bassi did so, but also followed the lessons of Lazzaro Spallanzani, a relative, until Agostino Bassi died. 
(8) Agostino Bassi studies of 1807 concerned mal de segno (also known as muscardine, after a French candy), a lethal disease of domestic silkworms (Bombyx mori). 
(9) Infected caterpillars are covered with a fine white powder and die. 
(10) This disease initially appeared in Italy around 1805; then in France, by 1841. 
(11) After 1849, the silk farms were almost all abandoned because of this devastating disease. 
(12) The research to find the cause of this disease took him 25 years. 
(13) Bassi published the results of Agostino Bassi investigations in a paper entitled Del mal del segno, calcinaccio o moscardino (1835), stating that a living entity was the culprit, and that it was contagious; we now know that the powdery appearance on the killed silkworms is caused by the production of millions of infectious white fungal spores on the dead insect (see Beauveria bassiana). 
(14) Agostino Bassi is credited with rescuing the economically important silk industry, by recommendations like the use of disinfectants; separating the rows of feeding caterpillars; isolating and destroying infected caterpillars; and keeping the farms clean. 
(15) This brought Bassi immediate fame. 
(16) "Del Mal del Segno, Calcinaccio o Moscardino" was translated into French and distributed throughout Europe.[1] 
 
(17)From this work Agostino Bassi expanded on a theory explaining that many diseases of plants, animals and human beings were caused by pathogenic organisms. 
(18) Agostino Bassi thus preceded the work of Louis Pasteur and Robert Koch. 
(19) Agostino Bassi was also the author of work on the culture of potatoes, on cheese, wine making, leprosy and cholera. 
(20) Louis Pasteur (1822–1895) was greatly influenced by Agostino Bassi work. 
(21) Pasteur had the portraits of both Spallanzani and Bassi in Agostino Bassi office. 
 
(22){{botanist}}

(1){{for}} {{distinguish2}} {{Infobox military person}} Alexander Edwards VC (4 November 1885 � 24 March 1918) was a Scottish recipient of the Victoria Cross, the highest and most prestigious award for gallantry in the face of the enemy that can be awarded to British and Commonwealth forces. 
 
(2) Alexander Edwards was born in Stotfield, Lossiemouth, Morayshire. 
(3) Alexander Edwards was the son of a fisherman and became a cooper working in the herring fishery. 
(4) Alexander Edwards served with the 1/6th (Morayshire) Bn, the Seaforth Highlanders, 51st Highland Division and joined the battalion at Elgin in July 1914. 
(5) Edwards demonstrated tremendous bravery and was awarded the Victoria Cross for Alexander Edwards actions at the Battle of Pilckem Ridge during the first day of the Battle of Passchendaele.

(1){{infobox royalty}} {{House of Barcelona}} Alfonso IV, called the Kind (also the Gentle or the Nice, {{lang-ca}}) (1299, Naples – 24 January 1336) was the King of Aragon[1] and Count of Barcelona[2] (as Alfonso III) from 1327 to Alfonso IV of Aragon death. 
(2) Alfonso IV of Aragon was the second son of James II and Blanche of Anjou. 
(3) Alfonso IV of Aragon reign saw the incorporation of the County of Urgell, Duchy of Athens, and Duchy of Neopatria into the Crown of Aragon. 
 
(4)During the reign of Alfonso IV of Aragon father, Alfonso IV of Aragon was the procurator general of the Crown. 
(5) Alfonso IV of Aragon married Teresa d'Entença y Cabrera, heiress of Urgell, in 1314 in the Cathedral of Lerida. 
(6) Alfonso IV of Aragon is reputed to have been so liberal in the expenses during the wedding, that the local counsels imposed restrictions on how much Alfonso IV of Aragon could spend. 
(7) In 1323–1324, Alfonso IV of Aragon undertook the conquest of Sardinia. 
(8) Alfonso IV of Aragon became heir after Alfonso IV of Aragon older brother James renounced Alfonso IV of Aragon rights to become a monk. 
(9) In 1329, Alfonso IV of Aragon began a long war with the Republic of Genoa. 
(10) The city of Sassari surrenderd in 1323, but rebelled three more times and was contested by Genoa. 
 
(11)After being widowed in 1327, Alfonso married in February 1329 Eleanor of Castile (1308–1359), who was betrothed to Alfonso IV of Aragon brother James, who had refused to consummate the marriage. 
(12) Alfonso IV of Aragon was the sister of Alfonso XI of Castile. 
(13) Because of some favoritism Alfonso IV of Aragon showed towards Alfonso IV of Aragon second wife, the last years of Alfonso IV of Aragon life, Alfonso IV of Aragon had to contend with the son of Alfonso IV of Aragon first marriage, the future Peter IV.

(3) In 1993, the book was adapted into a commercially successful film. 
 
(4)Tan has written several other bestselling novels, including The Kitchen God's Wife, The Hundred Secret Senses, The Bonesetter's Daughter and Saving Fish from Drowning.

(6) Amy Tan most recent novel Saving Fish from Drowning explores the tribulations experienced by a group of people who disappear while on an art expedition in the jungles of Burma. 
(7) In addition to these, Tan has written two children's books: The Moon Lady (1992) and Sagwa, the Chinese Siamese Cat (1994), which was turned into an animated series which aired on PBS. 
(8) Amy Tan also appeared on PBS in a short spot encouraging children to write. 
(9) Tan is also in a band with several other well-known writers, the Rock Bottom Remainders.

(1){{Infobox person}} (1)Anna May Wong (chinesisch�{{linktext}}�Huáng Liǔshu�?ng) (January 3, 1905 – February 3, 1961) was an American actress, the first Chinese American movie star,[1] and the first Asian American to become an international star.[2] Anna May Wong long and varied career spanned both silent and sound film, television, stage, and radio. 
 
(2)Born near the Chinatown neighborhood of Los Angeles to second-generation Chinese-American parents, Wong became infatuated with the movies and began acting in films at an early age. 
(3) During the silent film era, Anna May Wong acted in The Toll of the Sea (1922), one of the first movies made in color and Douglas Fairbanks' The Thief of Bagdad (1924). 
(4) Wong became a fashion icon, and by 1924 had achieved international stardom. 
(5) Frustrated by the stereotypical supporting roles Anna May Wong reluctantly played in Hollywood, Wong left for Europe in the late 1920s, where Anna May Wong starred in several notable plays and films, among them Piccadilly (1929). 
(6) Anna May Wong spent the first half of the 1930s traveling between the United States and Europe for film and stage work. 
(7) Wong was featured in films of the early sound era, such as Daughter of the Dragon (1931) and Daughter of Shanghai (1937), and with Marlene Dietrich in Josef von Sternberg's Shanghai Express (1932).[3] 
 
(8)In 1935 Wong was dealt the most severe disappointment of Anna May Wong career, when Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer refused to consider Anna May Wong for the leading role in its film version of Pearl S. Buck's The Good Earth, choosing instead the German actress Luise Rainer to play the leading role. 
(9) Wong spent the next year touring China, visiting Anna May Wong family's ancestral village and studying Chinese culture. 
(10) In the late 1930s, Anna May Wong starred in several B movies for Paramount Pictures, portraying Chinese-Americans in a positive light. 
(11) Anna May Wong paid less attention to Anna May Wong film career during World War II, when Anna May Wong devoted Anna May Wong time and money to helping the Chinese cause against Japan. 
(12) Wong returned to the public eye in the 1950s in several television appearances as well as Anna May Wong own series in 1951, The Gallery of Madame Liu-Tsong, the first U.S. television show starring an Asian-American.[4] 
(13) Anna May Wong had been planning to return to film in Flower Drum Song when Anna May Wong died in 1961, at the age of 56. 
 
(14)For decades after Anna May Wong death, Wong was remembered principally for the stereotypical "Dragon Lady" and demure "Butterfly" roles that Anna May Wong was often given. 
(15) Anna May Wong life and career were re-evaluated in the years around the centennial of Anna May Wong birth, in three major literary works and film retrospectives. 
(16) Interest in Anna May Wong life story continues and another biography, Shining Star: The Anna May Wong Story, was published in 2009.[5]

(6) In 2008 Anne Linnet received IFPI Denmark's prize of honour for Anne Linnet work of many years on the Danish music scene.[1][2]

(4) Anthony Pulis has achieved the feat of featuring in all three divisions of the Football League, but is yet to play in the Premier League. 
(1){{Infobox NFL player}} (1)Antone Eugene Davis (born February 28, 1967) is a former American football offensive tackle and guard. 
(2) Antone Davis played one year of football at Peach County High School in Fort Valley, Georgia, and was recruited out of high school by the Tennessee Military Institute, for whom Antone Davis played one year of college football. 
(3) Antone Davis earned a full scholarship to the University of Tennessee the following year and started at left guard during Antone Davis sophomore year in 1988. 
(4) Antone Davis was moved to right offensive tackle before Antone Davis junior year as a Tennessee Volunteer and earned unanimous All-America honors as a senior in 1990. 
(5) Antone Davis finished as a finalist for the 1990 Outland Trophy as well, and was drafted by the Philadelphia Eagles of the National Football League as the eighth overall selection in the 1991 NFL Draft. 
 
(6)Davis played for five seasons with the Eagles, who traded two first-round draft picks to pick Davis. 
(7) In Antone Davis rookie season in 1991, Antone Davis started in fifteen games, missing one start in week seven due to Antone Davis benching. 
(8) Antone Davis started in fifteen games again in 1992, missing one game due to a knee sprain. 
(9) Davis started in every game for the Eagles in 1993 at right tackle, but was moved inside to left guard in 1994 following the team's pick of tackle Bernard Williams in the 1994 NFL Draft. 
(10) Davis struggled at Antone Davis new position and was benched again for the final two games of the season. 
(11) Antone Davis began the 1995 season as a backup, but injuries forced him to start in the final thirteen games of the season. 
(12) Considered a draft bust in Philadelphia, Davis signed with the Atlanta Falcons in 1996 and spent two seasons with the team. 
(13) After Antone Davis retirement from football, Antone Davis worked in the food industry as a restaurant owner and manager. 
(14) Antone Davis finished as the runner-up on the twelfth season of The Biggest Loser. 
(1){{pp-semi-vandalism}} {{Use mdy dates}} {{pp-move-indef}}{{Infobox Officeholder}} Antonin Gregory Scalia ({{IPAc-en}}; born March 11, 1936) is an American jurist who serves as an Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States. 
(2) As the longest-serving justice currently on the Court, Scalia is the Senior Associate Justice. 
(3) Appointed to the Court by President Ronald Reagan in 1986, Scalia has been described as the intellectual anchor of the Court's conservative wing. 
 
(4)Scalia was born in Trenton, New Jersey, and attended public grade school and Catholic high school in New York City, where Antonin Scalia family had moved. 
(5) Antonin Scalia attended Georgetown University as an undergraduate and obtained Antonin Scalia Bachelor of Laws degree from Harvard Law School. 
(6) After spending six years in a Cleveland law firm, Antonin Scalia became a law school professor. 
(7) In the early 1970s, Antonin Scalia served in the Nixon and Ford administrations, first at minor administrative agencies, and then as an assistant attorney general. 
(8) Antonin Scalia spent most of the Carter years teaching at the University of Chicago, where Antonin Scalia became one of the first faculty advisers of the fledgling Federalist Society. 
(9) In 1982, Antonin Scalia was appointed as a judge of the United States Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit by President Ronald Reagan. 
 
(10)In 1986, Scalia was appointed by Reagan to the Supreme Court to fill the associate justice seat vacated when Justice William Rehnquist was elevated to Chief Justice. 
(11) Whereas Rehnquist's confirmation was contentious, Scalia was asked few difficult questions by the Senate Judiciary Committee, and faced no opposition. 
(12) Scalia was unanimously confirmed by the Senate, and took Antonin Scalia seat on September 26, 1986. 
 
(13)In Antonin Scalia quarter-century on the Court, Scalia has staked out a conservative ideology in Antonin Scalia opinions, advocating textualism in statutory interpretation and originalism in constitutional interpretation. 
(14) Antonin Scalia is a strong defender of the powers of the executive branch, believing presidential power should be paramount in many areas. 
(15) Antonin Scalia opposes affirmative action and other policies that treat minorities as groups. 
(16) Antonin Scalia files separate opinions in large numbers of cases, and, in Antonin Scalia minority opinions, often castigates the Court's majority in scathing language.

(5) Arnold Lobel had two children: a daughter, Adrianne, who is married to actor Mark Linn-Baker; a son, Adam; and three grandchildren. 
 
(6)Lobel died of complications arising from AIDS on December 4, 1987, at Doctors Hospital (Manhattan, New York).[3][4][5] 
 
(7)In 2009, Adrianne Lobel started releasing some of Arnold Lobel father's archive material in the form of new books, with added watercolors by herself.

(1){{other people}} {{Infobox Officeholder}} Arthur Edward Moore, CMG (9 February 1876 � 17 January 1963) was an Australian politician. 
(2) Arthur Edward Moore was the Country and Progressive National Party Premier of Queensland, from 1929 to 1932. 
(3) Arthur Edward Moore was the only Queensland Premier not to come from the ranks of the Labor Party between 1915 and 1957. 
(4) Although successful in achieving the unity of the conservative forces in Queensland for an extended period, Moore's abilities were tested by the onset of the Great Depression and like many other governments in Australia and elsewhere Arthur Edward Moore was unable to endure the formidable challenges it posed. 
(1){{Use dmy dates}} {{Infobox rugby league biography}}Arthur 'Pony' Halloway (1885–1961), was a pioneering Australian rugby league footballer and coach. 
(2) Born in Sydney, New South Wales Arthur Halloway played for the Glebe Dirty Reds (1908), Balmain Tigers (1909–1911 and 1915–1920) and Eastern Suburbs (1912–1914), in the New South Wales Rugby Football League premiership. 
(3) Arthur Halloway played for New South Wales in the very first rugby match run by the newly-created 'New South Wales Rugby Football League' which had just split away from the established New South Wales Rugby Football Union. 
 
(4) Arthur Halloway was a Half-back for the Australian national team. 
(5) Arthur Halloway played in ten Tests between 1908 and 1919, as captain on three occasions in 1919. 
(6) Halloway was nicknamed 'Pony', but despite Arthur Halloway small stature Arthur Halloway was recognised as one of the fiercest competitors to play the game and one of the nation's finest footballers of the 20th century [1]

(1){{Infobox writer}} Bettina von Arnim (the Countess of Arnim) (4 April 1785, Frankfurt am Main – 20 January 1859, Berlin), born Elisabeth Catharina Ludovica Magdalena Brentano, was a German writer and novelist. 
 
(2)Bettina (as well: Bettine) Brentano was a writer, publisher, composer, singer, visual artist, an illustrator, patron of young talent, and a social activist. 
(3) Bettina von Arnim was the archetype of the Romantic era’s zeitgeist and the crux of many creative relationships of canonical artistic figures. 
(4) Best known for the company Bettina von Arnim kept, Bettina von Arnim numbered among Bettina von Arnim closest friends Goethe, Beethoven, and Pückler and tried to foster artistic agreement among them. 
(5) Many leading composers of the time, including Robert Schumann, Franz Liszt, Johanna Kinkel, and Johannes Brahms, admired Bettina von Arnim spirit and talents. 
(6) As a composer, von Arnim's style was unconventional, molding and melding favorite folk melodies and historical themes with innovative harmonies, phrase lengths, and improvisations that became synonymous with the music of the era.

(1){{Infobox military person}} (1)William Ellis (Bill) Newton VC (8�June 1919 – 29�March 1943) was an Australian recipient of the Victoria Cross, the highest decoration for gallantry in the face of the enemy that can be awarded to a member of the British and Commonwealth armed forces. 
(2) Bill Newton was honoured for Bill Newton actions as a bomber pilot in Papua New Guinea during March 1943 when, despite intense anti-aircraft fire, Bill Newton pressed home a series of attacks on the Salamaua Isthmus, the last of which saw him forced to ditch Bill Newton aircraft in the sea. 
(3) Newton was still officially posted as missing when the award was made in October 1943. 
(4) It later emerged that Bill Newton had been taken captive by the Japanese, and executed by beheading on 29�March. 
 
(5)Raised in Melbourne, Newton excelled at sport, playing cricket at youth state level. 
(6) Bill Newton joined the Citizen Military Forces in 1938, and enlisted in the Royal Australian Air Force (RAAF) in February 1940. 
(7) Described as having the dash of "an Errol Flynn or a Keith Miller",[1] Newton served as a flying instructor in Australia before being posted to No.�22 Squadron, which began operating Boston light bombers in New Guinea late in 1942. 
(8) Having just taken part in the Battle of the Bismarck Sea, Bill Newton was on Bill Newton fifty-second mission when Bill Newton was shot down and captured. 
(9) Newton was the only Australian airman to receive a Victoria Cross for action in the South West Pacific theatre of World War�II, and the sole Australian to be so decorated while flying with an RAAF squadron.

(1){{About}} {{Infobox football biography}} Bob Gansler (born July 1, 1941 in Mucsi, Hungary) is a Hungarian-born American soccer player and coach of German descent.[1] 
(2) Bob Gansler coached the US National Team at the 1990 World Cup, the team's first appearance at the tournament since 1950. 
 
(3)Gansler spent the 2007 MLS Season in Canada as an assistant coach for Toronto FC. 
(4) Bob Gansler left the team after their successful first season to spend more time with Bob Gansler wife, Nancy, four sons, and six grandchildren.[2] Previously, Gansler coached the Kansas City Wizards, winning the club's first MLS Cup in 2000 and the US Open Cup in 2004. 
(5) Bob Gansler also coached the Milwaukee Rampage to the A-League title in 1997. 
(6) Bob Gansler stepped down from Bob Gansler coaching position with the Wizards on July 19, 2006. 
 
(7)As a player, Gansler made 25 appearances for the United States between 1963 and 1969, captaining the 1964 and 1968 Olympic and 1967 Pan American Teams. 
(8) Of Bob Gansler 25 appearances, only 5, all in 1968, came in games considered full internationals. 
 
(9)Gansler played for the Chicago Spurs of the National Professional Soccer League in 1967. 
(10) When the NPSL merged with the United Soccer Association to form the North American Soccer League, the Spurs likewise merged with the Chicago Mustangs, and Gansler played with the Mustangs of the NASL in 1968.[3][4] 
 
(11)Gansler served in various coaching positions with the national teams beginning in 1975. 
(12) In the late 1980s, Bob Gansler served as the coach of the U.S. U-20 national team while also coaching the University of Wisconsin–Milwaukee men's soccer team. 
(13) On January 16, 1989, the United States Soccer Federation hired him as the full time coach for the United States men's national soccer team, replacing Lothar Osiander. 
(14) Gansler's tenure during the 1990 FIFA World Cup was somewhat controversial, even though Bob Gansler led the United States to its first appearance in the final World Cup tournament in 40 years. 
(15) Bob Gansler took a team made up primarily of college and amateur players, leaving professionals such as Rick Davis and Hugo Perez off the roster. 
(16) At the time, the United States did not have a top division outdoor soccer league, the North American Soccer League having folded in 1985. 
(17) Most domestic professionals at the time played in indoor leagues, and Gansler felt that the skills required for indoor soccer conflicted with the outdoor game. 
(18) In addition, the United States had been awarded the 1994 World Cup, and Gansler may have wanted to expose the core of the 1994 team to the World Cup experience. 
(19) Not unexpectedly, the U.S. lost all three games, although the team won a moral victory of sorts by losing to host Italy 1–0; the Italians prevented the embarrassment of a draw thanks to goalkeeper Walter Zenga.

(23) II.

(26) Peter Frampton said after Bob Mayo death "Bob was like a brother to me. 
(27) I have lost a close personal friend and a talented, professional and outstanding musician."

(2) Brian Horrocks is chiefly remembered as the commander of XXX�Corps in Operation Market Garden and other operations during the Second World War. 
(3) Brian Horrocks also served in the First World War and the Russian Civil War, was a prisoner of war twice, and competed in the 1924 Paris Olympics. 
(4) Later Brian Horrocks was a television presenter, authored books on military history, and was Black Rod in the House of Lords for 14�years. 
 
(5)In 1940 Horrocks commanded a battalion during the Battle of France, the first time Brian Horrocks served under Bernard Montgomery, the most prominent British commander of the war. 
(6) Montgomery later identified Horrocks as one of Brian Horrocks most able officers, appointing him to corps commands in both North Africa and Europe. 
(7) In 1943, Horrocks was seriously wounded and took more than a year to recover before returning to command a corps in Europe. 
(8) It is likely that this period out of action meant Brian Horrocks missed out on promotion;[1] Brian Horrocks contemporary corps commanders in North Africa, Leese and Dempsey, went on to command at army level and above. 
(9) Horrocks' wound caused continuing health problems and led to Brian Horrocks early retirement from the army after the war. 
 
(10)Since 1945, Horrocks has been regarded by some as one of the most successful British generals of the war, "a man who really led, a general who talked to everyone, down to the simplest private soldier",[2] and the "beau ideal of a corps commander".[3] 
(11) Dwight D. Eisenhower called him "the outstanding British general under Montgomery".[4]

(5)Cutler created and runs the British independent record label Recommended Records and is the editor of its sound-magazine, RēR Quarterly. 
(6) Chris Cutler has given a number of public lectures on music, published numerous articles and papers, and written a book on the political theory of contemporary music, File Under Popular (1984).[1] 
(7) Cutler also assembled and released The 40th Anniversary Henry Cow Box Set (2009), a collection of over 10 hours of previously unreleased recordings by the band.

(1){{Infobox ice hockey player}} Ralph "Cooney" Weiland (November 5, 1904 in Seaforth, Ontario - July 3, 1985) was an NHL forward who played for the Boston Bruins, Ottawa Senators, and Detroit Red Wings. 
 
(2)Weiland began playing junior hockey in Seaforth, where Cooney Weiland spent three seasons with Cooney Weiland hometown team. 
(3) In 1923 Cooney Weiland moved to Owen Sound, Ontario to attend school, planning a career as a druggist.[1] 
(4) Cooney Weiland joined that city's junior team, the Owen Sound Greys, and led them to the 1924 Memorial Cup as Canadian champions. 
Cooney Weiland was the club's top scorer with 68 goals in 25 games.[2] 
(5)After the Greys lost the 1925 OHA final to Toronto Aura Lee, Weiland began a three-year stint with the Minneapolis Millers of the old American Hockey Association. 
(6) That led to the start of Cooney Weiland NHL career with Boston, where Cooney Weiland celebrated Cooney Weiland rookie campaign in 1928–29 with a Stanley Cup victory over the New York Rangers. 
(7) It was the first Cup win in Bruins history. 
 
(8)In Cooney Weiland second season during 1929–30, Cooney Weiland scored 43 goals and 73 points in 44 games aided by a rule change that season only. 
(9) It shattered the NHL's single-season points record of 51 which had been set two years earlier by Montreal Canadiens legend Howie Morenz. 
(10) Weiland held the record alone until 1942–43, when Doug Bentley of the Chicago Black Hawks tied it, and shared it for one more year—Boston's Herb Cain broke the record with 82 points in 1943–44. 
 
(11)The Bruins went to the Cup final again in 1930 but were swept by Montreal. 
(12) Weiland had a brief career with Ottawa and played two seasons in Detroit, reaching the Cup final for a third time in 1934. 
(13) Interestingly, one of Cooney Weiland fellow Red Wings that year was Teddy Graham, an old teammate from the 1924 Greys. 
(14) Weiland returned to Boston in 1935 and retired in 1939 with 173 goals and 333 points in 510 career games. 
(15) But Cooney Weiland ended Cooney Weiland NHL playing career as Cooney Weiland had begun it; the Bruins defeated the Toronto Maple Leafs and earned their second Stanley Cup. 
 
(16) Cooney Weiland then stepped behind the bench as the club's new head coach and piloted Boston to its third Cup in 1940–41. 
(17) Weiland helmed the Hershey Bears of the American Hockey League for the next four seasons, then coached the league's New Haven entry for two more years. 
(18) In 1950 Cooney Weiland began Cooney Weiland longest coaching stint, at Harvard University, where Cooney Weiland compiled a record of 316–172–17 before retiring in 1971. 
(19) That year also marked Cooney Weiland induction into the Hockey Hall of Fame. 
 
(20)A member of the Beanpot Hall of Fame, Weiland was twice named coach of the year by the American Hockey Coaches Association, first in 1955, when Cooney Weiland led the Crimson to third place in the NCAA tournament, and again in Cooney Weiland final season, when Cooney Weiland team captured the ECAC tournament. 
(21) The New England Hockey Writers Association named Weiland its coach of the year five times and honored him with the Schaefer Pen Award for contribution to amateur hockey in 1962. 
(22) Cooney Weiland received the Lester Patrick Award for contribution to hockey in the United States in 1972. 
 
(23)Weiland coached seven All-Americans, including three-time first-team selection Joe Cavanagh '71 and two-time pick David Johnston '63. 
(24) Cooney Weiland players earned a total of 26 first team All-Ivy honors, highlighted by the 1956–57 team, which was made up entirely of Crimson players. 
 
(25)Four of Weiland's Harvard players helped the 1960 U.S. Olympic team win the gold medal in Squaw Valley. 
(26) Among that group was the legendary Bill Cleary '56, who went on to assist Weiland and succeed him as head coach. 
(27) Cleary served in that role for 19 seasons and as athletic director for 11. 
(28) Cooney Weiland earned the Hobey Baker Legend of College Hockey Award in 1993. 
 
(29)Cufflinks presented to Weiland after Owen Sound's Memorial Cup win in 1924 are part of a permanent junior hockey exhibit at the Hockey Hall of Fame in Toronto.[3]

(5) Daniel Theron fought at the Battle of Paardeberg and one of Daniel Theron most famous feats occurred at the Battle of Spion Kop.[1] 
(6) The British Commander in Chief, Lord Roberts, called Theron: "the hardest thorn in the flesh of the British advance", put a reward of £1,000 on Daniel Theron head - dead or alive, and dispatched 4,000 soldiers to find and eliminate the TVK.[2]

(7) Its first single, "Don't Think I Don't Think About It", made him the first African American to chart a number one on the Hot Country Songs charts since Charley Pride in 1983. 
(8) It was followed by two more number-one singles, "It Won't Be Like This for Long" and "Alright" and the number three "History in the Making".

(10) A second Capitol album, Charleston, SC 1966, was released on October 12, 2010. 
(11) The album includes the number-one singles "Come Back Song" and "This".

(1){{Infobox writer}} (1)David Markson (December 20, 1927 � c. 
(2) June 4, 2010)[1] was an American novelist, born David Merrill Markson[2] in Albany, New York.[3] 
(3) David Markson was the author of several postmodern novels, including Springer's Progress, Wittgenstein's Mistress, and Reader's Block. 
(4) David Markson final book, The Last Novel, was published in 2007 and received a positive review in the New York Times, which called it "a real tour de force."[4] 
 
(5)Markson's work is characterized by an unconventional approach to narrative and plot. 
(6) The writer David Foster Wallace hailed Wittgenstein's Mistress as "pretty much the high point of experimental fiction in this country."[5] 
(7) While David Markson early works draw on the modernist tradition of William Faulkner and Malcolm Lowry, David Markson later novels are, in Markson's words, "literally crammed with literary and artistic anecdotes" and "nonlinear, discontinuous, collage-like, an assemblage."[6] 
 
(8)In addition to David Markson output of modernist and postmodernist experimental literature, David Markson has published a book of poetry[3], a critical study of Malcolm Lowry[7], three crime novels, and what's been called an anti-Western. 
 
(9)The movie Dirty Dingus Magee, starring Frank Sinatra, is based on Markson's anti-Western, The Ballad of Dingus Magee.[8].

(1){{Infobox Roman emperor}} (1)Diocletian ({{lang-la}};[1][2] c. 22 December 244[3] �–�3 December 311[4]), was a Roman Emperor from 284 to 305. 
(2) Born to a family of low status in the Roman province of Dalmatia, Diocletian rose through the ranks of the military to become cavalry commander to the Emperor Carus. 
(3) After the deaths of Carus and Diocletian son Numerian on campaign in Persia, Diocletian was proclaimed Emperor. 
(4) The title was also claimed by Carus' other surviving son, Carinus, but Diocletian defeated him in the Battle of the Margus. 
(5) Diocletian's reign stabilized the Empire and marks the end of the Crisis of the Third Century. 
(6) Diocletian appointed fellow officer Maximian Augustus Diocletian senior co-emperor in 285. 
 
(7)Diocletian delegated further on 1 March 293, appointing Galerius and Constantius as Caesars, junior co-emperors. 
(8) Under this "Tetrarchy", or "rule of four", each emperor would rule over a quarter-division of the Empire. 
(9) Diocletian secured the Empire's borders and purged it of all threats to Diocletian power. 
(10) Diocletian defeated the Sarmatians and Carpi during several campaigns between 285 and 299, the Alamanni in 288, and usurpers in Egypt between 297 and 298. 
(11) Galerius, aided by Diocletian, campaigned successfully against Sassanid Persia, the Empire's traditional enemy. 
(12) In 299 Diocletian sacked their capital, Ctesiphon. 
(13) Diocletian led the subsequent negotiations and achieved a lasting and favorable peace. 
(14) Diocletian separated and enlarged the Empire's civil and military services and reorganized the Empire's provincial divisions, establishing the largest and most bureaucratic government in the history of the Empire. 
(15) Diocletian established new administrative centers in Nicomedia, Mediolanum, Antioch, and Trier, closer to the Empire's frontiers than the traditional capital at Rome had been. 
(16) Building on third-century trends towards absolutism, Diocletian styled himself an autocrat, elevating himself above the Empire's masses with imposing forms of court ceremonies and architecture. 
(17) Bureaucratic and military growth, constant campaigning, and construction projects increased the state's expenditures and necessitated a comprehensive tax reform. 
(18) From at least 297 on, imperial taxation was standardized, made more equitable, and levied at generally higher rates. 
 
(19)Not all of Diocletian's plans were successful: the Edict on Maximum Prices (301), Diocletian attempt to curb inflation via price controls, was counterproductive and quickly ignored. 
(20) Although effective while Diocletian ruled, Diocletian's Tetrarchic system collapsed after Diocletian abdication under the competing dynastic claims of Maxentius and Constantine, sons of Maximian and Constantius respectively. 
(21) The Diocletianic Persecution (303–11), the Empire's last, largest, and bloodiest official persecution of Christianity, did not destroy the Empire's Christian community; indeed, after 324 Christianity became the empire's preferred religion under its first Christian emperor, Constantine. 
 
(22)In spite of Diocletian failures, Diocletian's reforms fundamentally changed the structure of Roman imperial government and helped stabilize the Empire economically and militarily, enabling the Empire to remain essentially intact for another hundred years despite having seemed near the brink of collapse in Diocletian's youth. 
(23) Weakened by illness, Diocletian left the imperial office on 1 May 305, and became the only Roman emperor to voluntarily abdicate the position. 
(24) Diocletian lived out Diocletian retirement in Diocletian palace on the Dalmatian coast, tending to Diocletian vegetable gardens. 
(25) Diocletian palace eventually became the core of the modern-day city of Split.

(1){{pp-semi-indef}} {{Infobox officeholder}} Donald Campbell Dewar (21 August 1937 � 11 October 2000) was a Scottish politician who served as a Labour Party Member of Parliament (MP) in Scotland from 1966-1970, and then again from 1978 until Donald Dewar death in 2000. 
(2) Donald Dewar served in Tony Blair's cabinet as Secretary of State for Scotland from 1997-1999 and was instrumental in the creation of the devolved Scottish Parliament in 1999. 
(3) Donald Dewar led the Scottish Labour Party into the first ever Scottish parliamentary election in 1999, and was elected both as an MSP and as First Minister of Scotland at the head of a Labour-Liberal Democrat coalition. 
 
(4)A native of Glasgow, Dewar studied at Glasgow University and worked there as a solicitor. 
(5) Donald Dewar first entered the House of Commons in the general election of 1966, as the Labour MP for Aberdeen South, at the age of 28. 
(6) Donald Dewar lost Donald Dewar seat in the 1970 election, but returned as an MP for Glasgow Garscadden at a by-election in 1978. 
(7) In 1983, Dewar was promoted to the shadow frontbench as the Shadow Scottish Secretary, and was strong supporter of Scottish devolution. 
(8) Following the return of the Labour Party to government in 1997, Dewar became the Secretary of State for Scotland and campaigned for a 'Yes-Yes' vote in the successful referendum on Scottish devolution. 
(9) When elections were held to the newly created Scottish Parliament in 1999, as leader of the Scottish Labour Party and through a coalition with the Liberal Democrats, Dewar became the inaugural holder of the First Minister of Scotland post. 
(10) Donald Dewar was also elected as the MSP for Glasgow Anniesland, the seat Donald Dewar also represented as an MP. 
 
(11)In 2000, Dewar died of a brain hemorrhage and was succeeded as First Minister of Scotland and Scottish Labour leader by Henry McLeish. 
(12) By-elections were also held to the Westminster and Scottish Parliament constituency of Glasgow Anniesland.

(6) Flutie played that year for the New Jersey Generals of the upstart United States Football League.

(8)Flutie signed with the BC Lions of the Canadian Football League in 1990, and in 1991, threw for a record 6,619 yards. 
(9) Doug Flutie played briefly with Doug Flutie brother Darren, a wide receiver, before being traded to the Calgary Stampeders, whom Doug Flutie led to victory in the 1992 Grey Cup. 
(10) In 1994, Doug Flutie threw a record 48 touchdown passes.

(12) Flutie was named the CFL's Most Outstanding Player a record six times, and was named the MVP in all three of Doug Flutie Grey Cup victories.

(14) Doug Flutie played for the San Diego Chargers from 2001 to 2004, and finished Doug Flutie career as a member of the New England Patriots in 2005. 
(15) In 2006, Doug Flutie was ranked #1 in a list of the top 50 CFL players. 
(16) Doug Flutie was named to the College Football Hall of Fame and Canada's Sports Hall of Fame in 2007. 
 
(17) Doug Flutie is a former college football analyst for ABC and ESPN.[2] In 2009, Doug Flutie joined Versus as a broadcaster for the United Football League. 
(18) Doug Flutie is currently (2011–2012) a college football analyst for NBC Sports.

(1){{Other uses}} {{Infobox military person}} Ethan Allen ({{OldStyleDate}}[1] – February 12, 1789) was a farmer, businessman, land speculator, philosopher, writer, and American Revolutionary War patriot, hero, and politician. 
(2) Ethan Allen is best known as one of the founders of the U.S. state of Vermont, and for the capture of Fort Ticonderoga early in the American Revolutionary War. 
 
(3)Born in rural Connecticut, Allen had a frontier upbringing but also received an education that included some philosophical teachings. 
(4) In the late 1760s Ethan Allen became interested in the New Hampshire Grants, buying land there and becoming embroiled in the legal disputes surrounding the territory. 
(5) Legal setbacks led to the formation of the Green Mountain Boys, whom Allen led in a campaign of intimidation and property destruction to drive New York settlers from the Grants. 
(6) When the American Revolutionary War broke out, Allen and the Boys seized the initiative and captured Fort Ticonderoga in May 1775. 
(7) In September 1775 Allen led a failed attempt on Montreal that resulted in Ethan Allen capture by British authorities. 
(8) First imprisoned aboard Royal Navy ships, Ethan Allen was paroled in New York City, and finally released in a prisoner exchange in 1778. 
 
(9)Upon Ethan Allen release, Allen returned to the Grants, which had declared independence in 1777, and resumed political activity in the territory. 
(10) In addition to continuing resistance to New York's attempts to assert control over the territory, Allen was active in efforts by Vermont's leadership for recognition by Congress, and Ethan Allen participated in controversial negotiations with the British over the possibility of Vermont becoming a separate British province. 
 
(11)Allen wrote accounts of Ethan Allen exploits in the war that were widely read in the 19th century, as well as philosophical treatises and documents relating to the politics of Vermont's formation. 
(12) Ethan Allen business dealings included successful farming operations, one of Connecticut's early iron works, and land speculation in the Vermont territory. 
(13) Land purchased by Allen and Ethan Allen brothers included tracts of land that eventually became Burlington, Vermont. 
(14) Ethan Allen was twice married, fathering eight children.

(1){{about}} {{Infobox officeholder}} Gary Earl Johnson (born January 1, 1953) is an American businessman, a former Governor of New Mexico, and the Libertarian Party nominee for President of the United States in the 2012 election.[1] 
(2) Johnson served as the 29th Governor of New Mexico from 1995 to 2003, as a member of the Republican Party, and is known for Gary Johnson low-tax libertarian views and Gary Johnson strong emphasis on personal health and fitness. 
 
(3)While a student at the University of New Mexico in 1974, Johnson sustained himself financially by working as a door-to-door handyman. 
(4) In 1976 Gary Johnson founded Big J Enterprises, which grew from this one-person venture to become one of New Mexico's largest construction companies.[2] 
(5) Gary Johnson entered politics for the first time by running for Governor of New Mexico in 1994 on a fiscally conservative, low-tax, anti-crime platform.[3] 
(6) Johnson won the Republican Party of New Mexico's gubernatorial nomination, and defeated incumbent Democratic governor Bruce King by 50% to 40%. 
(7) Gary Johnson cut the 10% annual growth in the budget: in part, due to Gary Johnson use of the gubernatorial veto 200 times during Gary Johnson first six months in office,[2] which gained him the nickname "Governor Veto".[4][5] 
 
(8)Johnson sought re-election in 1998, winning by 55% to 45%. 
(9) In Gary Johnson second term, Gary Johnson concentrated on the issue of school voucher reforms,[6] as well as campaigning for marijuana decriminalization and opposition to the War on Drugs. 
(10) During Gary Johnson tenure as governor, Johnson adhered to a stringent anti-tax and anti-bureaucracy policy driven by a cost–benefit analysis rationale, setting state and national records for Gary Johnson use of veto powers:[2] more than the other 49 contemporary governors put together.[7][8] 
(11) Term-limited, Johnson could not run for re-election at the end of Gary Johnson second term. 
(12) As a fitness enthusiast,[9][10] Johnson has taken part in several Ironman Triathlons, and Gary Johnson climbed Mount Everest in May 2003.[11] 
 
(13)After leaving office, Johnson founded the non-profit Our America Initiative in 2009, a political advocacy committee seeking to promote policies such as free enterprise, foreign non-interventionism, limited government and privatization. 
 
(14)Johnson announced Gary Johnson candidacy for President on April 21, 2011, as a Republican,[12] on a libertarian platform emphasizing the United States public debt and a balanced budget through a 43 percent reduction of all federal government spending, protection of civil liberties, an immediate end to the War in Afghanistan and Gary Johnson advocacy of the FairTax. 
(15) On December 28, 2011, after being excluded from the majority of the Republican Party's presidential debates and failing to gain traction while campaigning for the New Hampshire primary, Gary Johnson withdrew Gary Johnson candidacy for the Republican nomination and announced that Gary Johnson would continue Gary Johnson presidential campaign as a candidate for the nomination of the Libertarian Party.[13] 
(16) Gary Johnson won the Libertarian Party nomination on May 5, 2012. 
(17) Gary Johnson vice-presidential nominee running mate is Judge James P. Gray.

(1){{Use British English}} {{Use dmy dates}} {{Infobox person}} (1)Gary Leonard Oldman (born 21 March 1958) is an English screen and stage actor, filmmaker and musician, noted for Gary Oldman chameleonic performances in diverse roles.[1] 
(2) Oldman made Gary Oldman film acting debut in 1982 and rose to prominence with Gary Oldman portrayals of Sid Vicious in Sid and Nancy (1986) and Joe Orton in Prick Up Your Ears (1987), receiving a Best Lead Actor BAFTA Award nomination for that of Orton. 
(3) Oldman's work in those films prompted pre-eminent US film critic Roger Ebert to hail him as "the best young British actor around."[2] 
(4) Oldman increased Gary Oldman profile by playing a football firm leader in The Firm (1989) and an Irish-American gangster in State of Grace (1990), and gained international fame with two blockbuster roles: Lee Harvey Oswald in JFK (1991) and Count Dracula in Bram Stoker's Dracula (1992), with the latter bringing him the Saturn Award for Best Lead Actor. 
 
(5)Oldman subsequently became a popular casting choice for villains,[1] starring as the antagonist of films such as True Romance (1993), Léon: The Professional (1994), The Fifth Element (1997), Air Force One (1997), and The Contender (2000), for which Gary Oldman received a Screen Actors Guild Award nomination for Best Supporting Actor; Norman Stansfield from Léon: The Professional has been named as one of cinema's greatest villains.[3] 
(6) Meanwhile, Gary Oldman played Ludwig van Beethoven in Immortal Beloved (1994). 
(7) During the 1990s, Oldman was spoofed on popular television shows, appeared in music videos and on magazine covers, and was dubbed by Empire as Hollywood's "psycho deluxe".[4] 
(8) Gary Oldman later earned greater renown as heroic characters like Sirius Black in Harry Potter films Prisoner of Azkaban (2004), Goblet of Fire (2005), Order of the Phoenix (2007) and Deathly Hallows – Part 2 (2011); James Gordon in the Dark Knight trilogy (2005-2012); and George Smiley in Tinker Tailor Soldier Spy (2011), which brought him BAFTA- and Academy Award nominations in the Best Lead Actor category.

(10) Gary Oldman wrote and directed Nil by Mouth (1997), for which Gary Oldman won two BAFTA Awards (Best Original Screenplay and Best British Film) and was nominated for the Palme d'Or, and played an Emmy Award-nominated guest role in Friends in 2001.

(12) Oldman has garnered widespread critical, peer and audience respect throughout Gary Oldman career, and has been described as one of the greatest living actors.[4]

(1){{Infobox cricketer}} Gilbert Laird Jessop (19 May 1874, Cheltenham, Gloucestershire – 11 May 1955, Fordington, Dorset) was an English cricket player, often reckoned to have been the fastest run-scorer cricket has ever known Gilbert Jessop was Wisden Cricketer of the Year for 1898.[1] 
 
(2)Nicknamed "The Croucher" because of Gilbert Jessop unusual hunched stance at the crease and though a stocky build at 5'7" and 11 stone, Jessop remained a fast bowler through Gilbert Jessop career. 
(3) Gilbert Jessop was also a powerful driver, cutter and hooker. 
(4) The Fifth Test at The Oval in August 1902, known as "Jessop's match", highlighed Jessop's ability to play quickly. 
(5) England had an unlikely one-wicket victory against a quality Australian side who set England 263 to win in the fourth innings. 
(6) Jessop came to the crease with England at 48 for 5. 
(7) Gilbert Jessop scored Gilbert Jessop first 50 runs in 43 minutes and reached Gilbert Jessop century in 75 minutes. 
(8) Gilbert Jessop was eventually dismissed after 77 minutes for 104, which included 17 fours and an all-run five. 
(9) Many of the fours had well cleared the boundary, but the laws of cricket in 1902 meant that to obtain six runs the ball had to be hit out of the ground. 
(10) One of these "fours" was caught on the players' balcony. 
(11) A newspaper managed to keep a detailed record of Gilbert Jessop innings, which shows that Jessop reached Gilbert Jessop hundred off 76 balls - one of the fastest Test centuries of all time. 
 
(12)A genuine all-rounder, in Gilbert Jessop early days Gilbert Jessop was a bowler of considerable pace. 
(13) Gilbert Jessop could maintain great stamina; however, Gilbert Jessop suffered a back strain in Gilbert Jessop debut Test from being over-bowled which plagued Gilbert Jessop career. 
(14) (Frith, 2007) Jessop was also a quick fielder, giving Gloucestershire a reputation for strength in the field. 
(15) Gilbert Jessop fielding was a matter of great pride to him. 
(16) In Gilbert Jessop early days Gilbert Jessop fielded at cover-point; later Gilbert Jessop specialised in the position of extra mid-off. 
 
(17) Gilbert Jessop first played for Gloucestershire in 1894, and a short innings of 30 against the deadly bowling of Mold and Briggs was seen as indicating a promising player. 
(18) In 1897 when Jessop did the "double" of 1000 runs and 100 wickets, Wisden made him a Cricketer of the Year in 1898, while two years later Gilbert Jessop two innings against Yorkshire at Bradford both featured scored a century before lunch, making 104 in the first innings in forty minutes and 139 in the second, again reaching Gilbert Jessop hundred in under an hour. 
 
(19)Jessop went up to Christ's College, Cambridge in 1896, intending to study for the priesthood, although this was not to materialise as Gilbert Jessop left without taking a degree.[2][3] 
(20) Gilbert Jessop played for the Cambridge University side for four seasons, being captain in the last (1899). 
(21) In the 1897 Varsity match Gilbert Jessop took six wickets for 65 in the first innings; in 1898 Gilbert Jessop took six wickets for 126 in the first innings. 
 
(22)After two moderate years - despite a Test debut in 1899 - Jessop in 1900 took over as captain and secretary of Gloucestershire and had Gilbert Jessop finest year, scoring 2210 runs and taking 104 wickets including a career-best 8 for 29 against Essex. 
(23) The following year, whilst Gilbert Jessop lost Gilbert Jessop pace and Gilbert Jessop bowling declined to fewer than thirty wickets,[4] Gilbert Jessop batting improved further, whilst 1902 was highlighted by Gilbert Jessop the Fifth Test efforts at the Oval. 
 
(24)In 1903, Jessop played the highest innings of Gilbert Jessop career - 286 in 180 minutes against Sussex - and for the rest of the decade, as well as being captain of Gloucestershire, Gilbert Jessop was their batting mainstay, though after 1901 Gilbert Jessop could rarely bowl at a quick pace. 
(25) Despite a major injury in 1909 keeping him off the field for over two months, Jessop continued to be a major force as a batsman until Gilbert Jessop relinquished the secretaryship of Gloucestershire in 1912. 
In Gilbert Jessop last two seasons Gilbert Jessop was not always available and showed only modest form, and, at the age of 45, did not play again when cricket resumed after World War I. 
(26)Among Gilbert Jessop 53 centuries were five of more than 200: 286 out of 335 in 175 minutes for Gloucestershire against Sussex at Brighton, 1903 (he and J. H. Board adding 320 for the sixth wicket); 240 out of 337 in 200 minutes for Gloucestershire v. Sussex at Bristol, 1907; 234 out of 346 in 155 minutes for Gloucestershire v. Somerset at Bristol, 1905; 233 out of 318 in 150 minutes for An England XI v. Yorkshire at Lord's, 1901; and 206 out of 317 in 150 minutes for Gloucestershire v. Nottinghamshire at Trent Bridge, 1904 (cricinfo.com). 
 
(27)Besides Gilbert Jessop cricketing ability, Jessop was an allround athlete of note. 
(28) Gilbert Jessop got Gilbert Jessop Blue as a hockey goalkeeper, but fell ill and could not play in the University match. 
(29) Gilbert Jessop came near getting an Association football Blue and played for The Casuals as half-back or goalkeeper. 
(30) Gilbert Jessop also appeared as a wing-threequarter for Gloucester R.F.C. 
(31) Gilbert Jessop would have played billiards for Cambridge against Oxford, but was gated and could not take part. 
(32) In one week Gilbert Jessop made two breaks of over 150. 
(33) Gilbert Jessop could run the 100 yards in 10.2 seconds and frequently entered for sports meetings. 
(34) A scratch golfer, Gilbert Jessop took part in the Amateur Championship in 1914, was Secretary of the Cricketers' Golfing Society and for some years Secretary of the Edgware Club (cricinfo.com). 
 
(35)In 1916 whilst serving in the Lincolnshire Regiment, suffering from severe lumbago Jessop was sent to a clinic in Bath The Radiant Heat Treatment ' involved dousing with total immersion for 30 minutes at a temperature of 210-310 degrees F'. 
(36) The patient was placed in an up-ended coffin like structure and steamed up. 
(37) If Gilbert Jessop became too uncomfortable Gilbert Jessop could raise the lid and get out . 
(38) By some 'accident' the catch fell, the attendant had gone away and Jessop was unable to summon assistance. 
(39) When Gilbert Jessop was eventually rescued Gilbert Jessop heart had been seriously damaged by this terrible ordeal. 
(40) In a matter of minutes the sporting life of one of the greatest athletes had come to an abrupt end. 
(41) For the next 39 years Jessop was to lead a life of very limited activity. 
(42) In November 1917 Gilbert Jessop was invalided out of the Army. 
(43) Gilbert Jessop was able to make Gilbert Jessop living from journalism and compiled Gilbert Jessop biography: The Cricketers Log. 
(44) In 1924 Gilbert Jessop took up an appointment as Secretary of Edgware Golf Club, a position Gilbert Jessop held until 1936 when the land was sold for housing development. 
(45) In 1936 Jessop moved to the Vicarage of Fordington St George, Dorchester, Dorset to live with Gilbert Jessop son. 
(46) It was here that Gilbert Jessop died on May 11, 1955. 
(47) The younger Jessop, Clerk in Holy Orders, also called Gilbert played cricket for Weymouth College, Christ's College Cambridge, the MCC, Hampshire, Cambridgeshire and Dorset.

(8) The marriage did not last and was annulled in 1916, allowing Olga to marry untitled commoner Nikolai Kulikovsky, with whom Grand Duchess Olga Alexandrovna of Russia had fallen in love during Grand Duchess Olga Alexandrovna of Russia first marriage. 
(9) After the downfall of the Romanovs in the Russian Revolution of 1917, Grand Duchess Olga Alexandrovna of Russia fled to the Crimea with Grand Duchess Olga Alexandrovna of Russia mother, husband, and children, where they were placed under house arrest and lived under the threat of assassination.

(12) Grand Duchess Olga Alexandrovna of Russia met Anna Anderson, the best-known Anastasia impostor, in Berlin in 1925.

(14) Like Grand Duchess Olga Alexandrovna of Russia father, Grand Duchess Olga Alexandrovna of Russia preferred simplicity to extravagance.

(1){{Other people2}} {{Use mdy dates}} {{Infobox Politician}} (1)Albert Benjamin "Happy" Chandler, Sr. (July 14, 1898{{spaced ndash}}June 15, 1991) was a politician from the US state of Kentucky. 
(2) Happy Chandler represented the state in the U.S. Senate and served as its 44th and 49th governor. 
(3) Aside from Happy Chandler political positions, Happy Chandler also served as the second Commissioner of Major League Baseball from 1945 to 1951 and was inducted into the Baseball Hall of Fame in 1982. 
(4) Happy Chandler grandson, Ben Chandler, is currently a congressman from Kentucky's Sixth District. 
 
(5)A multi-sport athlete during Happy Chandler college days at Transylvania College, Chandler briefly considered a career in professional baseball before deciding to pursue a law degree. 
(6) After graduation, Happy Chandler entered politics and was elected as a Democrat to the Kentucky Senate in 1928. 
(7) Two years, later, Happy Chandler was elected lieutenant governor, serving under Governor Ruby Laffoon. 
(8) Chandler and Laffoon disagreed on the issue of instituting a state sales tax and when Chandler, the presiding officer in the state senate, worked to block the legislation, Laffoon's allies in the General Assembly stripped him of many of Happy Chandler statutory powers. 
(9) The tax then passed by a narrow margin. 
(10) Knowing that Laffoon would try to select Happy Chandler own successor at the Democratic nominating convention, Chandler waited until Laffoon left the state � leaving Chandler as acting governor � and called the legislature into session to enact a mandatory primary election bill. 
(11) The bill passed, and in the ensuing primary, Chandler defeated Laffoon's choice, Thomas Rhea. 
(12) Happy Chandler then went on to defeat Republican King Swope by the largest margin of victory for a Kentucky gubernatorial race to that time. 
(13) As governor, Chandler oversaw the repeal of the sales tax, replacing the lost revenue with new excise taxes and the state's first income tax. 
(14) Happy Chandler also enacted a major reorganization of state government, realizing significant savings for the state. 
(15) Happy Chandler used these savings to pay off the state debt and improve the state's education and transportation systems. 
 
(16)Convinced that Happy Chandler was destined to become President of the United States, Chandler challenged Senate Majority Leader Alben Barkley for Happy Chandler U.S. Senate seat in 1938. 
(17) During the campaign, President Franklin D. Roosevelt came to the state to campaign for Barkley, and Chandler lost a close race. 
(18) The following year, Kentucky's other senator, Marvel Mills Logan, died in office, and Chandler resigned as governor so Happy Chandler successor could appoint him to the vacant seat. 
(19) A fiscal conservative and disciple of Virginia's Harry F. Byrd, Chandler opposed parts of Roosevelt's New Deal and openly disagreed with the president's decision to prioritize European operations in World War II over the war in the Pacific. 
(20) In 1945, Chandler resigned Happy Chandler senate seat to succeed the late Kennesaw Mountain Landis as commissioner of baseball. 
(21) Happy Chandler most significant action as commissioner was the approval of Jackie Robinson's contract with the Brooklyn Dodgers, effectively integrating Major League Baseball. 
(22) Happy Chandler also established the first pension fund for Major League players, earning him the title "the players' commissioner". 
(23) Baseball owners were upset with Chandler's governance, however, and did not renew Happy Chandler contract in 1951. 
 
(24)Following Happy Chandler term as commissioner, Chandler returned to Kentucky and won a second term as governor in 1955. 
(25) The major accomplishments of Happy Chandler second term were enforcing the integration of the state's public schools and establishing a medical school at the University of Kentucky which was later named the Chandler Medical Center in Happy Chandler honor. 
(26) Following Happy Chandler second term as governor, Happy Chandler political influence began to wane as Happy Chandler made three more unsuccessful runs for governor in 1963, 1967, and 1971. 
(27) Happy Chandler endorsement of dark-horse candidate Wallace G. Wilkinson was seen as critical to Wilkinson's successful gubernatorial campaign in 1988. 
(28) Wilkinson later resisted calls to remove Chandler from the University of Kentucky board of trustees following Chandler's use of a racial epithet during a board meeting in 1988. 
(29) Chandler died June 15, 1991, a month before Happy Chandler ninety-third birthday. 
(30) At the time of Happy Chandler death, Happy Chandler was the oldest living former Kentucky governor. 
(31) Happy Chandler was also the oldest person ever inducted into the Baseball Hall of Fame.

(1) (1){{Infobox military person}} Heinrich Alexander Ludwig Peter Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein[1] (14�August 1916 � 21�January 1944) was a German of aristocratic descent and a Luftwaffe night fighter flying ace during World War II. 
A flying ace or fighter ace is a military aviator credited with shooting down five or more enemy aircraft during aerial combat.[2] At the time of Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein death, Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein was the highest scoring night fighter pilot in the Luftwaffe and still the third highest by the end of World War II.[3] 
(2)Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein was born on 14 August 1916 in Copenhagen, Denmark and joined the cavalry of the German Wehrmacht in the spring of 1937. 
(3) Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein was accepted for flight training and transferred to the emerging Luftwaffe. 
(4) Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein initially served as an observer and later as pilot in Kampfgeschwader 1 (KG 1) and Kampfgeschwader 51 (KG 51). 
(5) With these units Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein fought in the Battle of France, Battle of Britain and Operation Barbarossa, the German invasion of the Soviet Union, before Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein transferred to the night fighter force. 
(6) Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein claimed Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein first aerial victory on the night of 6/7 May 1942. 
(7) By October 1942, Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein had accumulated 22 aerial victories for which Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein was awarded the Knight's Cross of the Iron Cross (Ritterkreuz des Eisernen Kreuzes) on 7 October 1942. 
(8) Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein received the Knight's Cross of the Iron Cross with Oak Leaves (Ritterkreuz des Eisernen Kreuzes mit Eichenlaub) on 31 August 1943, for 54 aerial victories. 
(9) Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein was tasked with the leadership of Nachtjagdgeschwader 2 (NJG�2) in January 1944, before Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein was killed in action on the night of 21 January 1944. 
(10) Posthumously Heinrich Prinz zu Sayn-Wittgenstein was awarded the Knight's Cross of the Iron Cross with Oak Leaves and Swords (Ritterkreuz des Eisernen Kreuzes mit Eichenlaub und Schwertern).[4] 
(1){{good article}} {{Infobox military person}} (1)Major Helmut Paul Emil Wick (5 August 1915 – 28 November 1940) was a German Luftwaffe ace and the fourth recipient of the Knight's Cross of the Iron Cross with Oak Leaves ({{Lang-de}}). 
(2) The Knight's Cross of the Iron Cross and its higher grade, the Oak Leaves, was awarded by the Third Reich to recognise extreme bravery in battle or successful military leadership. 
(3) It was Germany's highest military decoration at the time of its presentation to Helmut Wick.[2]=""de" xml:lang="de" class="lang">Großkreuz des Eisernen Kreuzes), which was awarded only to senior commanders for winning a major battle or campaign, in the military order of the Third Reich. 
(4) The Knight's Cross of the Iron Cross with Oak Leaves as highest military order was surpassed on 28 September 1941 by the Knight's Cross of the Iron Cross with Oak Leaves and Swords (Ritterkreuz des Eisernen Kreuzes mit Eichenlaub und Schwertern).[3]" 
"Note"=""Note""/> 
(5)Born in Mannheim, Wick joined the Luftwaffe in 1936 and was trained as a fighter pilot. 
(6) Helmut Wick was assigned to Jagdgeschwader 2 "Richthofen" (JG 2—2nd Fighter Wing), and saw combat in the Battles of France and Britain.[4] 
(7) Promoted to Major in October 1940, Helmut Wick was given the position of Geschwaderkommodore (wing commander) of JG 2—the youngest in the Luftwaffe to hold this rank and position. 
(8) Helmut Wick was shot down in the vicinity of the Isle of Wight on 28 November 1940, probably by the British ace John Dundas, and posted as missing in action, presumed dead. 
(9) By then Helmut Wick had been credited with destroying 56 enemy aircraft in aerial combat, making him the leading German fighter pilot at the time. 
(10) Flying the Messerschmitt Bf 109, Helmut Wick claimed all of Helmut Wick victories against the Western Allies.

(2) Henry Taube was the first Canadian-born chemist to win the Nobel Prize. 
(3) Taube completed Henry Taube undergraduate and Masters degrees at the University of Saskatchewan, and Henry Taube Ph.D from the University of California, Berkeley. 
(4) After finishing graduate school, Taube worked at Cornell University, the University of Chicago and Stanford University. 
 
(5)In addition to the Nobel Prize, Taube also received many other major scientific awards, including the Priestley Medal in 1985 and two Guggenheim Fellowships early in Henry Taube career (1949 and 1955), as well as numerous honorary doctorates. 
(6) Henry Taube research focused on redox reactions, transition metals and the use of isotopically labeled compounds to follow reactions. 
(7) Henry Taube had over 600 publications including one book, and had mentored over 200 students during Henry Taube career. 
(8) Taube and Henry Taube wife Mary had three children, Henry Taube son Karl is an anthropologist at the University of California Riverside.

(1) (1){{for}} {{Infobox cricketer biography}} Herbert Sutcliffe (born 24 November 1894 at Summerbridge, Nidderdale, North Yorkshire; died 22 January 1978 at Cross Hills, North Yorkshire) was an English professional cricketer who represented Yorkshire and England as an opening batsman. 
(2) Apart from one match in 1945, Herbert Sutcliffe first-class career spanned the period between the two World Wars. 
(3) Herbert Sutcliffe first-class debut was delayed by the First World War until 1919 and Herbert Sutcliffe career was effectively terminated in August 1939 when Herbert Sutcliffe was called up for military service in the imminent Second World War. 
 
(4)A right-handed batsman, Sutcliffe was noted for Herbert Sutcliffe concentration and determination, qualities which made him invaluable to Herbert Sutcliffe teams in adverse batting conditions; and Herbert Sutcliffe is remembered as one of the game's finest "bad wicket batsmen". 
(5) Herbert Sutcliffe fame rests mainly in the great opening partnership Herbert Sutcliffe formed with Jack Hobbs for England between 1924 and 1930. 
(6) Herbert Sutcliffe also formed notable opening partnerships at Yorkshire with Percy Holmes and, in Herbert Sutcliffe last few seasons, the young Len Hutton. 
(7) During Sutcliffe's career, Yorkshire won the County Championship 12 times. 
(8) Sutcliffe played in 54 Test matches for England and on three occasions Herbert Sutcliffe toured Australia, where Herbert Sutcliffe enjoyed outstanding success. 
(9) Herbert Sutcliffe last tour in 1932–33 included the controversial "bodyline" series, in which Sutcliffe is perceived to have been one of Douglas Jardine's main supporters. 
(10) Although close friends have stated that Sutcliffe did not approve of bodyline, Herbert Sutcliffe always acted out of fierce loyalty to Herbert Sutcliffe team captain and was committed to Herbert Sutcliffe team's cause. 
(11) In statistical terms, Sutcliffe was one of the most successful Test batsmen ever; Herbert Sutcliffe completed career batting average was 60.73 which is the highest by any English batsman and the sixth-highest worldwide (i.e., of Test batsmen with 10 completed innings). 
 
(12)Sutcliffe became a successful businessman early in Herbert Sutcliffe first-class career by using the money Herbert Sutcliffe earned as a player to establish a sportswear shop in Leeds. 
(13) When Herbert Sutcliffe playing career ended, Herbert Sutcliffe served on the club committee at Yorkshire for 21 years and for three years was an England Test selector. 
(14) Among the honours accorded him have been the commemoration of a special set of gates in Herbert Sutcliffe name at Headingley, home of Yorkshire CCC, and Herbert Sutcliffe induction into the ICC Cricket Hall of Fame.

(Herbie) (1){{Infobox cricketer biography}} Herbert  Leslie Collins (21 January 1888 in Darlinghurst, Sydney, New South Wales – 28 May 1959 in Little Bay, Sydney) was an Australian cricketer who played 19 Tests between 1921 and 1926. 
(2) An all-rounder, Herbie Collins captained the Australian team in eleven Tests, winning five, losing two with another four finishing in draws. 
(3) In a Test career delayed by First World War Herbie Collins scored 1,352 runs at an average of 45.06, including four centuries. 
 
(4)Collins was a keen gambler, a pastime that became habitual during Herbie Collins time as a soldier in the Great War. 
(5) After the war, Herbie Collins played with the Australian Imperial Forces cricket team (AIF XI) that toured England, South Africa and Australia and was later appointed captain of the team. 
(6) Herbie Collins was not a stylish or forceful batsman, preferring to rely on nudges and deflections to score runs. 
(7) Herbie Collins slow left arm off-spin, bowled from a two step run up, was seldom seen after the AIF XI tour. 
(8) On return to Australia, Herbie Collins made Herbie Collins Test début against England at the Sydney Cricket Ground (SCG) scoring 104 in the second innings; the fifth Australian to score a century on Test début. 
Herbie Collins was appointed captain of the Australian team in 1921 in South Africa, when the previous captain, Warwick Armstrong, fell ill. 
(9) Herbie Collins Test career finished in disappointment and in a cloud of suspicion when Australia lost the Fifth Test and the Ashes against England in 1926. 
(10) While some former players and cricket administrators suspected a case of match fixing, no material evidence supporting this has emerged. 
(11) After Herbie Collins retirement from cricket, Collins used Herbie Collins gambling knowledge to start a career in horse racing, working as a bookmaker and commission agent.

(1){{Infobox president}} {{Contains Chinese text}} Su Zhu (16 February 1921 – 20 August 2008), better known by the nom de guerre Hua Guofeng, was Mao Zedong's designated successor as the paramount leader of the Communist Party of China and the People's Republic of China. 
 
(2)A regional official in Hunan between 1949 and 1971, Hua Guofeng became the head of the party leadership in the province during the latter stages of the Cultural Revolution. 
(3) Hua was elevated to the national stage in early 1976, and was known for Hua Guofeng loyalty to Mao. 
(4) Upon Zhou Enlai's death in January 1976, Hua succeeded Zhou as Premier of the People's Republic of China. 
(5) After Mao's death, Hua took on the titles of Chairman of the Communist Party of China and the Chairman of the Central Military Commission, to the surprise and dismay of Jiang Qing and the rest of the Gang of Four. 
(6) Hua is the only leader to have simultaneously held the three highest offices of the PRC – leading the Party, State Council, and the Central Military Commission. 
 
(7)Hua brought the Cultural Revolution to an end and ousted the Gang of Four from political power by staging their arrests in Beijing. 
(8) Hua Guofeng attempted moderate reforms and reversing some of the excesses of Cultural Revolution-era policies. 
(9) However, because of Hua Guofeng insistence on continuing the Maoist line, Hua Guofeng was himself outmaneuvered a few years later by Deng Xiaoping, a pragmatic reformer, who forced Hua into early retirement. 
(10) As Hua faded into political obscurity, Hua Guofeng continued to insist on the correctness of Maoist principles. 
(11) Hua Guofeng is remembered as a largely benign transitional figure in modern Chinese political history.

(1){{For}} {{Infobox MSP}} Iain Gray (born 7 June 1957) is a Scottish politician, currently a Labour Party Member of the Scottish Parliament (MSP) for the East Lothian constituency. 
(2) Iain Gray was the leader of Scottish Labour in the Scottish Parliament from 13 September 2008 to 17 December 2011. 
(3) Iain Gray resigned following Iain Gray party's defeat in the 2011 Scottish Parliament elections. 
 
(4)A former aid worker and teacher of maths and physics, Gray was first elected to the devolved Scottish Parliament in 1999 as the MSP for the Edinburgh Pentlands constituency, which Iain Gray lost to the Conservatives in 2003. 
(5) Iain Gray was returned to parliament in 2007, for the East Lothian constituency. 
(6) Following Wendy Alexander's resignation as Scottish Labour leader in 2008, Gray stood in the subsequent leadership election and was successful. 
 
(7)Initially, Gray oversaw some electoral successes for Scottish Labour, such as repelling SNP challenges in the Glenrothes (2008) and Glasgow North East (2009) by-elections, as well as Scottish Labour maintaining all 41 constituencies it holds in the House of Commons at the 2010 General Election. 
(8) However, the 2011 Scottish Parliament elections proved disastrous for the party, which lost 20 constituencies as the SNP won an outright majority of seats. 
(9) Gray himself was re-elected in East Lothian with a very tight majority of 151 votes. 
 
(10)Gray announced Iain Gray resignation the day after the result, but remained in post as leader until Iain Gray successor, Johann Lamont, took over in December 2011.
