(1){{Infobox writer}} Iordan Chimet (November 18, 1924 – May 23, 2006) was a Romanian poet, children's writer and essayist, whose work was inspired by Surrealism and Onirism. 
(2) Iordan Chimet is also known as a memoirist, theater, art and film critic, book publisher and translator. 
(3) Chimet, who was an opponent of totalitarianism in general and of the Communist regime in particular, was persecuted by the latter as a dissident, and lived much of Iordan Chimet life in obscurity. 
(4) Iordan Chimet experience as an employee of the cooperative society Centrocoop also made him one of the first professional copywriters in Iordan Chimet country. 
 
(5)The poems, fantasy works and fairy tales Iordan Chimet authored, although largely ignored locally upon being published, have since drawn acclaim for their accomplished style, and are considered by many unique in Romanian literature. 
(6) They explored the themes of innocence and melancholy, and have themselves been seen as a venue for a discreet advocacy of disobedience. 
(7) Chimet was also the author of critical essays on Latin American studies and Western or popular culture, and a publisher of anthologies on some of the major themes in Romanian society. 
(8) In addition, Iordan Chimet collected and published material on the life and legacy of playwright Mihail Sebastian, as well as on the history of the Romanian avant-garde. 
 
(9)Iordan Chimet had a lifelong friendship with Gheorghe Ursu, a dissident who was killed by the Securitate secret police in 1985, and with science fiction author Camil Baciu. 
(10) Iordan Chimet was also a friend of the German writer Michael Ende and the Greek poet Odysseas Elytis, with whom Iordan Chimet kept in touch in spite of the difficulties posed by their living on different sides of the Iron Curtain.

(1){{Use dmy dates}} {{Infobox officeholder}} Major-General Sahibzada Sayyid Iskander Ali Mirza, CIE, OBE English IPA: ɪskɑndæɾ əɪi�? mi(ə)ɹzə (Urdu: اسکندر مرزا; Bengali: ইস�?কান�?দার মীর�?জা; 14 December 1898 – 12 November 1969), was the first President of Pakistan, serving from 1956 until being forced out from the presidency in 1958.[1] 
(2) Prior to that, Mirza was the last Governor-General of Pakistan from 1955 until 1956.[2] A great-grandson of the last Nawab of Bengal Mir Jafar,[3] Mirza was the first president of Bengali origin from East-Pakistan, and a retired career army officer, having reached the prestigious higher rank of major-general in Pakistan Army. 
 
(3)Starting Iskander Mirza career in the government, Mirza was employed by Prime minister Liaquat Ali Khan in the Ministry of Defence, being appointed as the first Defence Secretary (one of an apex bureaucratic post), overseeing the Indo-Pakistan war of 1947, and the Balochistan conflict in 1948.[4] 
(4) Serious disorder and civil unrest sparked in East Pakistan as a result of the Bengali Language Movement in 1952, prompting Prime minister Khawaja Nazimuddin to appoint him as the Governor of the province. 
(5) Iskander Mirza oversaw the success of the One Unit programme in East Pakistan in 1954, and succeeded Malik Ghulam as the Governor-General in 1955. 
(6) After successfully promulgating the 1956 constitution, Mirza became the first president. 
(7) But Iskander Mirza presidency saw great political instability, challenges in foreign policy, and the ousture of four prime ministers in two years. 
(8) Iskander Mirza finally imposed martial law in 1958 after suspending the constitution and dissolving democratic institutions, including the Pakistan Parliament. 
(9) Mirza has the distinction of being the first to bring in military influence in national politics after Iskander Mirza appointed Iskander Mirza army chief as chief martial law administrator of the country. 
 
(10)Problems with the Pakistan Armed Forces and the establishment escalated after relations with the United States deteriorated. 
(11) Imposition of martial law only led to more civil unrest and political challenges, and also undermined Mirza's own position. 
(12) After only twenty days of martial law, Mirza was forced out of the presidency by Iskander Mirza Chief Martial Law Administrator Field Marshal Ayub Khan. 
(13) Iskander Mirza was exiled to London, where Iskander Mirza resided until Iskander Mirza death. 
(14) When Iskander Mirza died in 1969 after a long illness, President Yahya Khan denied him a burial in Pakistan. 
(15) Out of respect, the Shah Mohammad Reza Pahlavi gave him a state funeral in Tehran, Iran.

(1){{Infobox person}} (1)Jafar Panahi ({{lang-fa}}; born July 11, 1960 Meyaneh, East Azarbaijan) is an Iranian film director, screenwriter and film editor most commonly associated with the Iranian New Wave film movement. 
(2) After several years of making short films and working as an assistant director for fellow Iranian filmmaker Abbas Kiarostami, Panahi first achieved international recognition with Jafar Panahi feature film debut The White Balloon in 1995. 
(3) The film won the Caméra d'Or at the 1995 Cannes Film Festival, which was the first major award won by an Iranian film at Cannes. 
(4) Panahi was quickly recognized as one of the most influential filmmakers in Iran. 
(5) Although Jafar Panahi films were often banned in Jafar Panahi own country, Jafar Panahi continued to receive international acclaim from film theorists and critics and has won numerous awards, including the Golden Leopard at the 1997 Locarno International Film Festival for The Mirror, the Golden Lion at the 2000 Venice Film Festival for The Circle and the Silver Bear for Best Director at the 2006 Berlin Film Festival for Offside.[1] 
(6) Jafar Panahi films are known for their humanistic perspective on life in Iran, often focusing on the hardships of children, the impoverished and women. 
(7) Author Hamid Dabashi has said that "Panahi does not do as Jafar Panahi is told — in fact Jafar Panahi has made a successful career in not doing as Jafar Panahi is told."[2] 
 
(8)After several years of conflict with the Iranian government over the content of Jafar Panahi films (including several short-term arrests), Panahi was arrested in March 2010 along with Jafar Panahi wife, daughter and 15 friends and was later charged with committing propaganda against the Iranian government. 
(9) Despite support from filmmakers, film organizations and human rights organizations from around the world, in December 2010 Panahi was sentenced to a six-year jail sentence and a 20-year ban on directing any movies, writing screenplays, giving any form of interview with Iranian or foreign media and from leaving the country.[3] 
(10) This led to Panahi's last film to date: This Is Not a Film, a documentary feature in the form of a video diary that was made despite of the legal ramifications of Panahi's arrest. 
(11) It was smuggled out of Iran in a Flash-Drive hidden inside a cake and was screened at the 2011 Cannes Film Festival.

(2) With James McCudden six British medals and one French one, McCudden received more medals for gallantry than any other airman of British nationality serving in the First World War. 
(3) James McCudden was also one of the longest serving, having joined the Royal Flying Corps in 1913. 
(4) McCudden's story is all the more remarkable as James McCudden rose through the RFC ranks (from Air Mechanic to Major) during the war to become one of the most decorated and honoured soldiers of the conflict.

(1){{Use dmy dates}} {{Infobox military person}} Major General James P. Wolfe (2 January 1727 – 13 September 1759) was a British Army officer, known for James Wolfe training reforms but remembered chiefly for James Wolfe victory over the French in Canada. 
(2) The son of a distinguished general, Lieutenant-General Edward Wolfe, James Wolfe had received James Wolfe first commission at a young age and saw extensive service in Europe where James Wolfe fought during the War of the Austrian Succession. 
(3) James Wolfe service in Flanders and in Scotland, where James Wolfe took part in the suppression of the Jacobite Rebellion, brought him to the attention of James Wolfe superiors. 
(4) The advancement of James Wolfe career was halted by the Peace Treaty of 1748 and James Wolfe spent much of the next eight years in garrison duty in the Scottish Highlands. 
(5) Already a brigade major at the age of eighteen, James Wolfe was a lieutenant-colonel by the age of twenty-three. 
 
(6)The outbreak of the Seven Years' War in 1756 offered Wolfe fresh opportunities for advancement. 
(7) James Wolfe part in the aborted attack on Rochefort in 1757 led William Pitt to appoint him second-in-command of an expedition to capture Louisbourg. 
(8) Following the success of this operation James Wolfe was made commander of a force designated to sail up the Saint Lawrence River to capture Quebec. 
(9) After a lengthy siege Wolfe defeated a French force under Montcalm allowing British forces to capture the city. 
(10) Wolfe was killed at the height of the battle due to injuries from three musket balls. 
 
(11)Wolfe's part in the taking of Quebec in 1759 earned him posthumous fame and James Wolfe became an icon of Britain's victory in the Seven Years War and subsequent territorial expansion. 
(12) James Wolfe was depicted in the painting The Death of General Wolfe. 
(13) This painting became very famous around the world. 
(14) Wolfe was posthumously dubbed "The Hero of Quebec", "The Conqueror of Quebec", and also "The Conqueror of Canada" since the capture of Quebec led directly to the capture of Montreal which ended French control of the country.

(7) With the decrease of the demand for big-breasted blonde bombshells and the increase in the negative backlash against Jayne Mansfield over-publicity, Jayne Mansfield became a box-office has-been by the end of the 1960s. 
(8) Jayne Mansfield career declined first to low-budget foreign movies and major Las Vegas nightclub dates; then to television guest appearances; next to touring plays and minor Las Vegas nightclub dates; and finally ended in small nightclub dates.[3]

(7)In addition to acting, Lange is a photographer with two published works,[2] and is a humanitarian, holding a position as a Goodwill Ambassador for UNICEF, specializing in the HIV/AIDS epidemic in the Congo and Russia.[3][4]

(4) During the 1930s, Jessica Tandy appeared in a large number of plays in London's West End, playing roles such as Ophelia, opposite John Gielgud's legendary Hamlet, and Katherine, opposite Laurence Olivier's Henry V.[1] In the 1930s, Jessica Tandy also worked in a couple of British films. 
(5) Following the end of Jessica Tandy marriage to the British actor Jack Hawkins, Jessica Tandy moved to New York in 1940, where Jessica Tandy met Canadian actor Hume Cronyn. 
(6) Jessica Tandy became Jessica Tandy second husband and frequent partner on stage and screen. 
 
(7) Jessica Tandy won the Tony Award for Jessica Tandy performance as Blanche Dubois in the original Broadway production of A Streetcar Named Desire in 1948, sharing the prize with Katherine Cornell (who won for the female lead in Antony and Cleopatra) and Judith Anderson (for the latter's portrayal of Medea). 
(8) Over the following three decades, Jessica Tandy career continued sporadically and included a supporting role in Alfred Hitchcock's horror film, The Birds (1963), and a Tony Award-winning performance in The Gin Game (1977, playing in the two-hander play opposite Hume Cronyn). 
(9) Along with Cronyn, Jessica Tandy was a member of the original acting company of the Guthrie Theater. 
 
(10)In the mid-1980s Jessica Tandy had a career revival. 
(11) Jessica Tandy appeared with Cronyn in the Broadway production of Foxfire in 1983 and its television adaptation four years later, winning both a Tony Award and an Emmy Award for Jessica Tandy portrayal of Annie Nations. 
(12) During these years, Jessica Tandy appeared in films such as Cocoon (1985), also with Cronyn. 
 
(13) Jessica Tandy became the oldest actress to receive the Academy Award for Best Actress for Jessica Tandy role in Driving Miss Daisy (1989), for which Jessica Tandy also won a BAFTA and a Golden Globe, and was nominated for an Academy Award for Best Supporting Actress for Fried Green Tomatoes (1991). 
(14) At the height of Jessica Tandy success, Jessica Tandy was named as one of People's "50 Most Beautiful People". 
(15) Jessica Tandy was diagnosed with ovarian cancer in 1990, and continued working until shortly before Jessica Tandy death.

(1){{Other people}} {{Infobox scientist}} John Couch Adams (5 June 1819 – 21 January 1892) was a British mathematician and astronomer. 
(2) Adams was born in Laneast, near Launceston, Cornwall, and died in Cambridge. 
(3) The Cornish name Couch is pronounced "cooch". 
 
(4) John Couch Adams most famous achievement was predicting the existence and position of Neptune, using only mathematics. 
(5) The calculations were made to explain discrepancies with Uranus's orbit and the laws of Kepler and Newton. 
(6) At the same time, but unknown to each other, the same calculations were made by Urbain Le Verrier. 
(7) Le Verrier would assist Berlin Observatory astronomer Johann Gottfried Galle in locating the planet on 23 September 1846, which was found within 1° of its predicted location, a point in Aquarius. 
(There was, and to some extent still is, some controversy over the apportionment of credit for the discovery; see Discovery of Neptune.) 
(8) John Couch Adams was Lowndean Professor at the University of Cambridge for thirty-three years from 1859 to John Couch Adams death. 
(9) John Couch Adams won the Gold Medal of the Royal Astronomical Society in 1866. 
(10) In 1884, John Couch Adams attended the International Meridian Conference as a delegate for Britain. 
 
(11)A crater on the Moon is jointly named after him, Walter Sydney Adams and Charles Hitchcock Adams. 
(12) Neptune's outermost known ring and the asteroid 1996 Adams are also named after him. 
(13) The Adams Prize, presented by the University of Cambridge, commemorates John Couch Adams prediction of the position of Neptune. 
(14) John Couch Adams personal library is now in the care of Cambridge University Library.

(1){{Infobox artist}} (1)John Lewis Krimmel (May 30, 1786-July 15, 1821), sometimes called "the American Hogarth" was America's first painter of genre scenes. 
(2) Born in Germany, John Lewis Krimmel emigrated to Philadelphia in 1809 and soon became a member of the Pennsylvania Academy of Fine Arts. 
(3) Initially influenced by Scotland's David Wilkie, England's William Hogarth and America's Benjamin West, John Lewis Krimmel soon turned to direct observation of life for John Lewis Krimmel genre scenes. 
(4) John Lewis Krimmel was among the first artists in America to portray free blacks, such as in Black People's Prayer Meeting (1813). 
(5) Among John Lewis Krimmel still frequently reproduced paintings are Fourth of July, Center Square (1811/12) and Election Day (1815), both filled with lively characterizations of scores of crowd members. 
(6) Krimmel died in a swimming accident at the age of 35. 
(7) Among the prominent American artists influenced by Krimmel's work are William Sidney Mount, George Caleb Bingham, and Thomas Eakins.

(1){{Use dmy dates}} {{Infobox military person}} (1)Marshal of the Royal Air Force Sir John Cotesworth (Jack) Slessor GCB, DSO, MC (3 June 1897 � 12 July 1979) was a senior commander in the Royal Air Force (RAF), serving as Chief of the Air Staff from 1950 to 1952. 
(2) As a pilot in the Royal Flying Corps during World War I, John Slessor saw action with No. 17 Squadron in the Middle East, earning the Military Cross, and with No. 5 Squadron on the Western Front, where John Slessor was awarded the Belgian Croix de Guerre. 
(3) Between the wars John Slessor commanded No. 4 Squadron in England, and No. 3 (Indian) Wing, earning the Distinguished Service Order for operations with the latter in Waziristan. 
(4) In 1936, John Slessor published Air Power and Armies, examining of the use of air power against targets on and behind the battlefield. 
 
(5)Slessor held several operational commands in World War II. 
(6) As Air Officer Commanding Coastal Command in 1943 and 1944, John Slessor was credited with doing "much to start winning the Battle of the Atlantic" by John Slessor use of long-range bombers against German U-boats. 
(7) John Slessor was knighted in June 1943. 
(8) In the closing stages of the war John Slessor became Commander-in-Chief RAF Mediterranean and Middle East and deputy to Lieutenant General Ira Eaker as Commander-in-Chief Mediterranean Allied Air Forces, conducting operations in the Italian Campaign and Yugoslavia. 
(9) Slessor went on to serve in the RAF's most senior post, Chief of the Air Staff, in the early 1950s, and was considered a strong proponent of strategic bombing and the nuclear deterrent. 
(10) In retirement John Slessor published two more books, including an autobiography, and held appointments with Somerset County Council.

(1){{about}} {{Infobox military person}} (1)Joseph Hooker (November 13, 1814 � October 31, 1879) was a career United States Army officer, achieving the rank of major general in the Union Army during the American Civil War. 
(2) Although Joseph Hooker served throughout the war, usually with distinction, Hooker is best remembered for Joseph Hooker stunning defeat by Confederate General Robert E. Lee at the Battle of Chancellorsville in 1863. 
 
(3)After graduating from the United States Military Academy in 1837, Hooker served in the Seminole Wars and the Mexican-American War, receiving three brevet promotions. 
(4) Resigning from the Army in 1853, Joseph Hooker pursued farming, land development, and (unsuccessfully) politics in California. 
(5) After the start of the Civil War Joseph Hooker returned to the Army as a brigadier general. 
(6) Joseph Hooker distinguished himself as an aggressive combat commander leading a division in the Battle of Williamsburg, May 5, 1862, resulting in Joseph Hooker promotion to major general. 
(7) As a corps commander, Joseph Hooker led the initial Union attacks at the Battle of Antietam, in which Joseph Hooker was wounded. 
(8) At the Battle of Fredericksburg, Joseph Hooker commanded a "Grand Division" of two corps, and was ordered to conduct numerous futile frontal assaults that caused Joseph Hooker men to suffer serious losses. 
(9) Throughout this period, Joseph Hooker conspired against and openly criticized Joseph Hooker army commanders. 
(10) Following the defeat at Fredericksburg, Joseph Hooker was given command of the Army of the Potomac. 
 
(11)Hooker planned an audacious campaign against Robert E. Lee, but Joseph Hooker was defeated by the Confederate Army at the Battle of Chancellorsville. 
(12) Hooker suddenly lacked the nerve to marshal the strength of Joseph Hooker larger army against Lee, who boldly divided Joseph Hooker army and routed a Union corps with a flank attack by Stonewall Jackson. 
(13) Hooker began to pursue Lee at the start of the Gettysburg Campaign, but Joseph Hooker poor performance at Chancellorsville prompted Abraham Lincoln to relieve him from command just prior to the Battle of Gettysburg. 
(14) Joseph Hooker returned to combat in November, leading two corps from the Army of the Potomac to help relieve the besieged Union Army at Chattanooga, Tennessee, and achieving an important victory at the Battle of Lookout Mountain during the Chattanooga Campaign. 
(15) Joseph Hooker continued in the Western Theater under Maj. 
(16) Gen. William T. Sherman, but left before the end of the Atlanta Campaign when Joseph Hooker was bypassed for a promotion to command the Army of the Tennessee. 
 
(17)Hooker became known as "Fighting Joe" following a journalist's clerical error reporting from the Battle of Williamsburg; however, the nickname stuck. 
(18) Joseph Hooker personal reputation was as a hard-drinking ladies' man, and Joseph Hooker headquarters was known for parties and gambling, although the historical evidence discounts any heavy drinking by the general himself. 
(19) Joseph Hooker name is often associated with the slang term for prostitute, although the etymology of that word has been documented to appear in print well before Joseph Hooker became a public figure.

(8) Kate Beckinsale will next appear in the legal drama The Trials of McCall. 
 
(9)Born and raised in London, Beckinsale is the child of actor Richard Beckinsale (1947–1979) and actress Judy Loe. 
(10) Kate Beckinsale had an eight-year relationship with Welsh actor Michael Sheen from 1995 until 2003; they have one daughter. 
(11) Kate Beckinsale married American film director Len Wiseman in 2004 and they live in Brentwood, Los Angeles. 
(12) Publications such as Esquire and People Magazine have repeatedly included Beckinsale in their annual rankings of the world's sexiest and most beautiful women.

(4) Since 1997 Kenneth Clarke has been President of the Tory Reform Group. 
 
(5)Clarke was a minister throughout the 18 years of successive Conservative governments from 1979 to 1997, serving in the cabinets of both Margaret Thatcher and John Major. 
(6) After the Conservative defeat in the 1997 general election Clarke became a backbencher. 
(7) Kenneth Clarke has contested the Conservative Party leadership three times—in 1997, 2001 and 2005—and was defeated each time. 
(8) Although Kenneth Clarke is considered popular with the general public, Kenneth Clarke pro-European integration views conflict with the modern Conservative Party's EU-sceptic stance. 
(9) Notably, Kenneth Clarke is President of the Conservative Europe Group and Vice-President of the European Movement UK.[1] 
(10) Despite this conflict, and Kenneth Clarke involvement with the tobacco industry, Conservative leader David Cameron returned Clarke to the Shadow Cabinet in March 2009 as Shadow Business Secretary. 
(11) When Cameron became Prime Minister in May 2010 Kenneth Clarke appointed Clarke Lord Chancellor and Secretary of State for Justice.

(1){{Infobox person}} (1)Kenule "Ken" Beeson Saro Wiwa (10 October 1941 – 10 November 1995) was a Nigerian author, television producer, environmental activist, and winner of the Right Livelihood Award and the Goldman Environmental Prize. 
(2) Saro-Wiwa was a member of the Ogoni people, an ethnic minority in Nigeria whose homeland, Ogoniland, in the Niger Delta has been targeted for crude oil extraction since the 1950s and which has suffered extreme and unremediated environmental damage from decades of indiscriminate petroleum waste dumping. 
(3) Initially as spokesperson, and then as President, of the Movement for the Survival of the Ogoni People (MOSOP), Saro-Wiwa led a nonviolent campaign against environmental degradation of the land and waters of Ogoniland by the operations of the multinational petroleum industry, especially the Royal Dutch Shell company. 
(4) Ken Saro-Wiwa was also an outspoken critic of the Nigerian government, which Ken Saro-Wiwa viewed as reluctant to enforce environmental regulations on the foreign petroleum companies operating in the area. 
 
(5)At the peak of Ken Saro-Wiwa non-violent campaign, Saro-Wiwa was arrested, hastily tried by a special military tribunal, and hanged in 1995 by the military government of General Sani Abacha, all on charges widely viewed as entirely politically motivated and completely unfounded. 
(6) Ken Saro-Wiwa execution provoked international outrage and resulted in Nigeria's suspension from the Commonwealth of Nations for over three years.

(1){{Use dmy dates}} {{Infobox person}} (1)Khwaja Ahmad Abbas ({{lang-hi}}) (7 June 1914 – 1 June 1987), popularly known as K. A. Abbas, was an Indian film director, novelist, screenwriter, and a journalist in the Urdu, Hindi and English languages. 
(2) Khwaja Ahmad Abbas was the maker of important Hindi films such as Saat Hindustani (1969) and Do Boond Pani (1972), both of which won the National Film Award for Best Feature Film on National Integration, Palme d'Or nominated (Cannes Film Festival) Pardesi (1957) and Shehar Aur Sapna (1963), which won the National Film Award for Best Feature Film. 
 
(3)As a screenwriter, Khwaja Ahmad Abbas is considered one of pioneers of Indian parallel or neo-realistic cinema, having penned films like the Palme d'Or winner at the Cannes Film Festival, Neecha Nagar (1946), Jagte Raho, Dharti Ke Lal, Awara, Saat Hindustani and Naya Sansar. 
(4) Apart from this, Khwaja Ahmad Abbas wrote the best of Raj Kapoor films, Awaara, Shri 420, Mera Naam Joker, Bobby and Henna.[1] 
 
(5) Khwaja Ahmad Abbas column ‘Last Page’, holds the distinction of being one of the longest-running columns in the history of Indian journalism. 
(6) The column began in 1935, at Bombay Chronicle, and when it closed, it moved to the Blitz, where it continued till Khwaja Ahmad Abbas death in 1987.[2] 
(7) Khwaja Ahmad Abbas was awarded the Padma Shri in 1969, by Government of India.

(1){{Infobox writer}} (1)Krste Petkov Misirkov ({{lang-bg}}; {{lang-mk}}) (born 18 November 1874 in Postol, Ottoman Empire (today Pella, Greece); died 26 July 1926 in Sofia, Kingdom of Bulgaria) was a philologist, slavist, historian, ethnographer, publicist author of the first book and scientific magazine in Macedonian[1][2][3] where Krste Misirkov for the first time outlined the principles of the literary Macedonian language.[4][5][6] 
(2) Krste Misirkov is one of the most outstanding names in the Macedonian culture and history.[7] 
 
(3)A part of Krste Misirkov writings is central to the development of ethnic Macedonian nationalism, the issue of the existence of a Macedonian nation distinct from the Bulgarian nation. 
(4) Krste Misirkov published one book, one magazine and over thirty articles. 
(5) However, at different points in Krste Misirkov life, Misirkov expressed conflicting statements about the ethnicity of the Slavic population living in Macedonia, including Krste Misirkov own ethnicity, calling them Bulgarians, Macedonians or Macedonian Bulgarians respectively. 
(6) As a result, both Krste Misirkov ethnic attachment and legacy remains a matter of dispute among some historians from Bulgaria and Republic of Macedonia.

(1){{Infobox military person}} (1)Technical Sergeant Leonard P. Matlovich (July 6, 1943 – June 22, 1988)[1] was a Vietnam War veteran, race relations instructor, and recipient of the Purple Heart and the Bronze Star.[2] (1)Matlovich was the first gay service member to purposely out himself to the military to fight their ban on gays, and perhaps the best-known gay man in America in the 1970s next to Harvey Milk. 
(2) Leonard Matlovich fight to stay in the United States Air Force after coming out of the closet became a cause célèbre around which the gay community rallied. 
(3) Leonard Matlovich case resulted in articles in newspapers and magazines throughout the country, numerous television interviews, and a television movie on NBC. 
(4) Leonard Matlovich photograph appeared on the cover of the September 8, 1975, issue of Time magazine, making him a symbol for thousands of gay and lesbian servicemembers and gay people generally.[3][4][5][6] 
(5) Matlovich was the first openly gay person to appear on the cover of a U.S. newsmagazine.[7] 
(6) According to author Randy Shilts, "It marked the first time the young gay movement had made the cover of a major newsweekly. 
(7) To a movement still struggling for legitimacy, the event was a major turning point." 
(8) [8] In October 2006, Matlovich was honored by LGBT History Month as a leader in the history of the LGBT community.

(1){{Unreferenced}} {{Infobox musical artist}} (1)Leroy Vinnegar (July 13, 1928 – August 3, 1999) was an American jazz bassist. 
 
(2)Born in Indianapolis, the self-taught Vinnegar established Leroy Vinnegar reputation in Los Angeles during the 1950s and 1960s. 
(3) Leroy Vinnegar trademark was the rhythmic "walking" bass line, a steady series of ascending or descending notes, and it brought him the nickname "The Walker". 
(4) Besides Leroy Vinnegar jazz work, Leroy Vinnegar also appeared on a number of soundtracks and pop albums, notably Van Morrison's 1972 album, Saint Dominic's Preview. 
 
(5) Leroy Vinnegar recorded extensively as both a leader and sideman. 
(6) Leroy Vinnegar came to public attention in the 1950s as a result of recording with Lee Konitz, André Previn, Stan Getz, Shorty Rogers, Chet Baker, Shelly Manne, Joe Castro and Serge Chaloff. 
(7) Leroy Vinnegar played bass on Previn and Manne's My Fair Lady album, one of the most successful jazz records ever produced. 
(8) Leroy Vinnegar also performed on another of jazz's biggest hit albums, Eddie Harris and Les McCann's Swiss Movement, released in 1969. 
 
(9) Leroy Vinnegar moved to Portland, Oregon in 1986. 
(10) In 1995, the Oregon State Legislature honored him by proclaiming May 1 Leroy Vinnegar Day. 
 
(11)Vinnegar died from a heart attack, at the age of 71, on August 3, 1999, in a hospital in Portland. 
(1){{Infobox person}} Levi Coffin (October 28, 1798 – September 16, 1877) was an American Quaker, abolitionist, and businessman. 
(2) Coffin was deeply involved in the Underground Railroad in Indiana and Ohio and Levi Coffin home is often called "Grand Central Station of the Underground Railroad". 
(3) Levi Coffin was nicknamed "President of the Underground Railroad" because of the thousands of slaves that are reported to have passed through Levi Coffin care while escaping their masters. 
 
(4)Born in the Southern United States, Coffin was exposed to and developed an opposition to slavery as a child. 
(5) Levi Coffin followed Levi Coffin family and immigrated to Indiana from North Carolina in 1826 following a persecution of the Quakers by the slave-holders. 
(6) In Indiana Levi Coffin quickly became a local business leader as a merchant and farmer. 
(7) The wealth Levi Coffin accumulated allowed him to become a major investor in the Richmond branch of the Bank of Indiana where Levi Coffin served as director during the 1830s. 
(8) Levi Coffin position in the community allowed him to provide most of the funds necessary to supply food, clothing, and transportation for the Underground Railroad operations in Levi Coffin region. 
 
(9)At the urging of friends in the anti-slavery movement, Levi Coffin moved to Cincinnati in 1847 to operate a warehouse selling only goods produced by free labor. 
(10) Despite making considerable progress with the business, the venture was unprofitable and Levi Coffin was forced to abandon the enterprise in 1857. 
(11) Throughout the period, Levi Coffin continued to assist hundreds of runaway slaves by lodging them in Levi Coffin Ohio home. 
(12) After slavery was abolished following the American Civil War, Coffin traveled around the Midwestern United States and abroad to France and Great Britain where Levi Coffin was instrumental in forming aid societies to provide food, clothing, funds, and education to the freed slaves. 
(13) Levi Coffin retired during the 1870s and wrote an autobiography that was published a year before Levi Coffin death. 
(1){{Infobox President}} (1)Lê Duẩn (7 April 1907 – 10 July 1986) was a Vietnamese communist politician. 
(2) Le Duan rose in the party hierarchy in the late 1950s, and was elected General Secretary of the Central Committee of the Communist Party of Vietnam at the 3rd Party Congress. 
(3) Lê continued Hồ Chí Minh's policy of ruling through collective leadership. 
(4) From 1958 until Le Duan death in 1986, Le Duan was the top decision-maker in Vietnam. 
 
(5)Born into a lower-class family in Quảng Trị province, French Indochina as Lê Văn Nhuận; little is known about Lê's family and childhood. 
(6) Le Duan first came in contact with Marxist literature in the 1920s through Le Duan work as a railway clerk. 
(7) Lê was a founding member of the Indochina Communist Party (the future Communist Party of Vietnam) in 1930, but was arrested the following year, in 1931. 
(8) Le Duan spent six years in jail until Le Duan was released in 1937. 
(9) From 1937 to 1939 Le Duan worked himself up the party ladder; but Le Duan was rearrested in 1939, this time for fomenting an uprising in the South. 
(10) Lê was released from jail following the successful communist-led August Revolution. 
 
(11)During the First Indochina War, Lê was active in a communist cadre in the South. 
(12) Le Duan headed the Central Office of South Vietnam, a Communist Party organ, from 1951 until 1954. 
(13) During the 1950s Lê became increasingly aggressive towards the South and called for reunification through war. 
(14) By the mid-to-late 1950s Lê had become the second-most powerful policy-maker within the Communist Party, eclipsing former party First Secretary Trư�?ng Chinh. 
(15) By 1960, at the 3rd Party Congress, Le Duan had become General Secretary and, because of Le Duan office, officially become the second-most powerful party member, after Hồ Chí Minh, the party Chairman. 
(16) Throughout the 1960s Hồ's health began to decline, and Lê took over more of Le Duan responsibilities. 
(17) On 2 September 1969, Hồ died and Lê became the most powerful decision-maker in Vietnam. 
 
(18)Throughout the Vietnam War, Lê had held an aggressive posture against the Americans. 
(19) Attack, rather than a defensive strategy, was to be the key to victory. 
(20) When Vietnam finally won the war in 1975, Lê and Le Duan associates were overly optimistic about the future. 
(21) The Second Five-Year Plan (1976–1980) was a failure, and after it the Vietnamese economy was in dire crisis. 
(22) To make matters worse, Vietnam was headed by a gerontocracy (in which the rulers are much older than the population average). 
(23) Vietnam became internationally isolated during Lê's rule; the country had invaded Cambodia and ousted Pol Pot from power (which was internationally condemned), engaged in a short war with China and had become dependent on Soviet economic aid. 
(24) Lê died in 1986 and was succeeded by Trư�?ng in July 1986; Trư�?ng was in turn succeeded by Nguyễn Văn Linh in December later that year.

(1){{about}} {{cleanup-tense}} {{infobox superhero}} Lois Lane is a fictional character appearing in comic book published by DC Comics. 
(2) Lois Lane is the primary love interest of Superhero Superman. 
(3) Like Superman's alter ego, Clark Kent, Lois Lane is a reporter for the Metropolis newspaper "The Daily Planet". 
(4) Created by writer Jerry Siegel and artist Joe Shuster, Lois Lane first appeared in Action Comics #1 (June 1938). 
(5) Lois is Superman's chief romantic interest and, for fifteen years in the DC continuity, was Lois Lane wife. 
 
(6)Lois's character was created from many influences, Lois Lane physical appearance was originally based on Joanne Carter, a model hired by Jerry Siegel and Joe Shuster who would later marry Siegel. 
(7) The character's personality was based on Torchy Blane, a female reporter featured in a series of films from the 1930s. 
(8) Siegel took Lois Lane name from actress Lola Lane, who portrayed Torchy in one of the middle entries.[1] 
(9) Lois Lane is also based on real life journalist Nellie Bly. 
 
(10)Depictions of Lois Lane have varied since Lois Lane character was created in 1938, spanning the 70-year history of Superman comics and other media adaptations. 
(11) During the Silver Age, Lois Lane was the star of Superman's Girl Friend, Lois Lane, a comic title that had a light and frivolous tone. 
(12) However, the original Golden Age version of Lois, as well as versions of Lois Lane from the 1970s onwards, portrays Lois as a tough-as-nails journalist and intellectual equal to Superman. 
(13) One thing has remained throughout the character's 70-year history, Lois Lane has always been the most prominent love-interest in Superman's life and is seen by many fans as the archetypical comic book love interest. 
 
(14)The character was ranked 78th in Comics Buyer's Guide's "100 Sexiest Women in Comics" list.[2]

(1){{Infobox artist}} Marcel Janco ({{IPA-de}}, {{IPA-fr}}, common rendition of the Romanian name Marcel Hermann Iancu[1] {{IPA-ro}}, last name also Ianco, Janko or Jancu; May 24, 1895 – April 21, 1984) was a Romanian and Israeli visual artist, architect and art theorist. 
(2) Marcel Janco was the co-inventor of Dadaism and a leading exponent of Constructivism in Eastern Europe. 
(3) In the 1910s, Marcel Janco co-edited, with Ion Vinea and Tristan Tzara, the Romanian art magazine Simbolul. 
(4) Janco was a practitioner of Art Nouveau, Futurism and Expressionism before contributing Marcel Janco painting and stage design to Tzara's literary Dadaism. 
(5) Marcel Janco parted with Dada in 1919, when Marcel Janco and painter Hans Arp founded a Constructivist circle, Das Neue Leben. 
 
(6)Reunited with Vinea, Marcel Janco founded Contimporanul, the influential tribune of the Romanian avant-garde, advocating a mix of Constructivism, Futurism and Cubism. 
(7) At Contimporanul, Janco expounded a "revolutionary" vision of urban planning. 
(8) Marcel Janco designed some of the most innovative landmarks of downtown Bucharest. 
(9) Marcel Janco worked in many art forms, including illustration, sculpture and oil painting. 
 
(10)Janco was one of the leading Romanian Jewish intellectuals of Marcel Janco generation. 
(11) Targeted by antisemitic persecution before and during World War II, Marcel Janco emigrated to British Palestine in 1941. 
(12) Marcel Janco won the Dizengoff Prize and Israel Prize, and was a founder of Ein Hod, a utopian art colony, controversially built over a deserted Palestinian Arab settlement. 
 
(13)Marcel Janco was the brother Georges and Jules Janco, who were Marcel Janco artistic partners during and after the Dada episode. 
(14) Marcel Janco brother-in-law and fellow Constructivist promoter was the writer Jacques G. Costin, known as a survivor of 1940s antisemitism.

(1){{Infobox scientist}} Mario Renato Capecchi (Verona, Italy, 6 October 1937) is an Italian-born American molecular geneticist and a co-winner of the 2007 Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine for discovering a method to create mice in which a specific gene is turned off (knocked out). 
(2) Mario Capecchi infected embryonic stem cells with a viral vector that was designed to incorporate itself into the target gene by homologous recombination. 
(3) Homologous recombination is a process that cells normally use to repair their DNA, which is exploited by viruses to infect cells. 
(4) Mario Capecchi shared the prize with Martin Evans and Oliver Smithies.[1] 
(5) Mario Capecchi is currently Distinguished Professor of Human Genetics and Biology at the University of Utah School of Medicine, which Mario Capecchi joined in 1973.

(1){{Infobox scientist}} (1)Mark Kac (pronounced kahts, Polish: Marek Kac, b. 3 August 1914, Krzemieniec, Russian Empire, now in Ukraine; d. 26 October 1984, California, USA) was a Polish mathematician.[1] 
(2) Mark Kac main interest was probability theory. 
(3) Mark Kac question, "Can one hear the shape of a drum?" set off research into spectral theory, with the idea of understanding the extent to which the spectrum allows one to read back the geometry. 
(4) (In the end, the answer was "no", in general.) 
 
(5)Kac completed Mark Kac Ph.D. in mathematics at the Polish University of Lwów in 1937 under the direction of Hugo Steinhaus.[2] 
(6) While there, Mark Kac was a member of the Lwów School of Mathematics. 
(7) After receiving Mark Kac degree Mark Kac began to look for a position abroad, and in 1938 was granted a scholarship from the Parnas Foundation which enabled him to go work in the United States. 
(8) Mark Kac arrived in New York City in November, 1938.[3] 
(9) With the onset of World War II, Kac was able to remain in America, while Mark Kac parents and brother who remained in Poland were murdered by the Germans in the mass executions in Krzemieniec (1942-43) for being Jewish.[4] 
(10) From 1939 until 1961 Mark Kac was at Cornell University, first as an instructor, then from 1943 as assistant professor and from 1947 as full professor.[5] 
(11) While there, Mark Kac became a naturalized US citizen in 1943. 
(12) In 1961 Mark Kac left Cornell and went to Rockefeller University in New York City. 
(13) After twenty years there, Mark Kac moved to the University of Southern California where Mark Kac spent the rest of Mark Kac career.

(1){{Infobox US Cabinet official}} (1)Marshall Jewell (October 20, 1825{{spaced ndash}}February 10, 1883) was a political figure who served as 44th and 46th Governor of Connecticut, the U.S. Minister to Russia, and the 25th United States Postmaster General. 
(2) Jewell, distinguished for Marshall Jewell fine "china" skin, grey eyes, and white eyebrows, was popularly known as the "Porcelain Man".[1] 
(3) As Postmaster General, Jewell was intent on reforming the Postal Service from internal corruption and profiteering, however, President Grant became suspicious of Jewell's loyalty after Jewell fired a Boston postmaster over non payment of a surety bond.[1] 
(4) Postmaster Jewell aided Secretary of the Treasury Benjamin H. Bristow shut down and prosecute the Whiskey Ring. 
 
(5)A native of New Hampshire, Jewell was the son of a prominent tanner and currier; having apprenticed in Marshall Jewell father's tannery business.[2] Jewell moved to Boston where Marshall Jewell learned the art of being a currier. 
(6) In 1847, Jewell moved to Hartford where Marshall Jewell worked for Marshall Jewell father's business as a currier. 
(7) Jewell stopped working as a currier and became a skilled telegrapher, where Marshall Jewell worked in New York, Ohio, and Tennessee. 
(8) Jewell was a Whig who supported the election of Zachary Taylor to the office of the Presidency. 
(9) Having supported Taylor, Jewell moved to Mississippi where Marshall Jewell was elected General Superintendent of Telegraphers.[2] Jewell moved back to New York in 1849, and in 1850 Marshall Jewell returned to Marshall Jewell father's tannery business having entered into partnership with Marshall Jewell father. 
(10) Between 1859 and 1860, Jewell traveled to and visited Europe on business connected with the tannery firm, having returned to the United States during the onset of the American Civil War. 
In 1865 Jewell returned to Europe and traveled to Egypt and the Holy Land.[2] 
(11)Having returned to the United States, Jewell, a Republican, ran for Connecticut state senator in 1867, however, Marshall Jewell failed to win the election.[3] 
(12) In 1868, Jewell ran for the office of Connecticut Governor, however, Marshall Jewell lost the election.[3] 
(13) Jewell ran again the following year and was elected Governor of Connecticut having served from 1869 until 1870. 
(14) Jewell was reelected to the governorship in 1871 having served until 1873. 
(15) In 1873, Jewell was appointed Consul to Russia by President Ulysses S. Grant having served until 1874 when Marshall Jewell was appointed by President Grant to Postmaster General to the United States; having served until 1876. 
(16) Jewell was also a presidential candidate at the 1876 Republican National Convention and served as the chairman of the Republican National Committee from 1880 until 1883. 
(17) Having returned to Connecticut, Jewell became a wealthy merchant, having invested in the Hartford Evening Post and the Southern New England Telephone Company.[1][3] 
(18) Marshall Jewell died in 1883 in New Haven, Connecticut, and was interred at Cedar Hill Cemetery in Hartford, Connecticut.[4]

(3) Prior to that, Martin Dempsey served as Commanding General, U.S. Army Training and Doctrine Command, from December 8, 2008, to April 11, 2011, as Acting Commander, U.S. Central Command, from March 24, 2008, to October 30, 2008, as Deputy Commander, U.S. Central Command, from August 2007 to March 23, 2008, and as Commanding General, Multi-National Security Transition Command – Iraq (MNSTC-I), from August 2005 to August 2007. 
(4) As the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, Dempsey is currently the highest ranking military officer in all of the United States Armed Forces. 
(5) Dempsey assumed Martin Dempsey current assignment on October 1, 2011.[1]

(4) Indeed, the true horror of Marvin's existence is that no task Marvin the Paranoid Android could be given would occupy even the tiniest fraction of Marvin the Paranoid Android vast intellect. 
(5) Marvin claims Marvin the Paranoid Android is 50,000 times more intelligent than a human,[2] (or 30 billion times more intelligent than a live mattress) though this is, if anything, a vast underestimation. 
(6) When kidnapped by the bellicose Krikkit robots and tied to the interfaces of their intelligent war computer, Marvin simultaneously manages to plan the entire planet's military strategy, solve "all of the major mathematical, physical, chemical, biological, sociological, philosophical, etymological, meteorological and psychological problems of the Universe except Marvin the Paranoid Android own, three times over," and compose a number of lullabies. 
(7) Marvin the Paranoid Android seemed to find this last task the hardest, and only one, "How I Hate the Night", is known.{{Clarify}} 
 
(8)Marvin's voice was performed by Stephen Moore on radio and television, while Alan Rickman voiced this role in the film. 
(9) David Learner operated Marvin the Paranoid Android body on television, having previously played and voiced the part for the stage version, and Warwick Davis wore the Marvin costume for the feature film. 
(10) A recreation of the costume from the 1981 television series makes an appearance in the film, as one of the robots standing in a queue on Vogsphere, where the main characters are trying to release Tricia.

(1){{BLP sources}} {{Infobox musical artist}} (1)Mervyn Edwin Warren (born February 29, 1964) is an American film composer, record producer, music conductor, music arranger, lyricist, songwriter, pianist, and vocalist. 
(2) Warren is a five-time Grammy Award winner and a 10-time Grammy Award nominee. 
(3) Warren has written the underscore and songs for many feature and television films and has written countless arrangements in a variety of musical styles for producers Quincy Jones, David Foster, Arif Mardin, and dozens of popular recording artists, including extensive work on Jones' Back on the Block, Q's Jook Joint, and Q: Soul Bossa Nostra. 
 
(4)Warren has also produced numerous jazz, pop, R&B, contemporary Christian, and gospel artists, typically arranging those recordings, often performing on them (on piano, keyboards, or vocals), and often writing or co-writing the melodies and lyrics. 
(5) Warren is best known as an original member of the a cappella vocal group Take 6,[1] for having composed the underscore to the #1 film The Wedding Planner, for producing and arranging songs for the hit film Sister Act 2, and for producing and arranging most of the soundtrack to the Whitney Houston film The Preacher's Wife -- the best-selling "gospel" album of all time.

(5) A salary reputed to be as high as $8 million per year[1] made Gascoyne the highest paid engineer in Formula One, eclipsing far more successful Technical Directors such as Adrian Newey and Ross Brawn. 
 
(6)Gascoyne's highly confrontational and aggressive management style has earned him the nickname "the rottweiler".[2] 
(6)Gascoyne currently lives in Oxfordshire with Mike Gascoyne post-divorce partner Silvi and Mike Gascoyne children Joel, Connie and Freddie Gascoyne.

(1){{Infobox scientist}} (1)Nader Engheta (Born October 8, 1955 in Tehran) is an Iranian-American scientist. 
(2) Nader Engheta is currently the H. Nedwill Ramsey Professor at the University of Pennsylvania, Philadelphia, Pennsylvania, USA, affiliated with the departments of Electrical and Systems Engineering, Bioengineering, and Physics and Astronomy. 
 
(3) Nader Engheta has made significant contributions to the fields of metamaterials, transformation optics, plasmonic optics, nanophotonics, graphene photonics, nano-materials, nanoscale optics, nanoantennas and miniaturized antennas, physics and reverse-engineering of polarization vision in nature, bio-inspired optical imaging, fractional paradigm in electrodynamics, and electromagnetics and microwaves.[1][2] 
[3] 
(4) Nader Engheta is the originator of the new area of optical metatronics, i.e., metamaterial-based optical nano circuitry, in which properly designed nano structures function as "lumped' optical circuit elements such as optical capacitors, optical inductors and optical resistors. 
(5) [4] [5] [6]. 
(6) These are the building blocks for the metatronic circuits operating with light. 
(7) This concept has been recently verified and realized experimentally by him and Nader Engheta research group at the University of Pennsylvania. 
(8) [7] This provides a new circuit paradigm for information processing at the nanoscale. 
 
(9) Nader Engheta and Nader Engheta group have pioneered and developed several exciting areas and concepts in the fields of metamaterials and plasmonic optics, including, (1) ‘extreme-parameter metamaterials’ and 'epsilon-near-zero (ENZ) metamaterials' [8]; (2) the concept of Omega structures, as one of the building blocks of structured materials [9], [10]; (3) ultrathin cavities and waveguides, with sizes beyond diffraction limits, providing possibilities for unprecedented miniaturization of devices [11]; (4) supercoupling phenomena between waveguides using low-permittivity ENZ metamaterials [12], [13]; (5) extended Purcell effects in nano-optics using the ENZ phenomena, in which enhanced photon density of states occurs in a relatively large area with essentially uniform phase [14]; (6) far-field subwavelength imaging lens based on ENZ hyperbolic metamaterials [15]; (7) scattering-cancellation-based plasmonic cloaking and transparency [16], [17]; (8) merging the field of graphene with the field of metamaterials and plasmonic optics in infrared regime, providing the roadmaps for one-atom-thick optical devices and one-atom-thick information processing [18], [19]; (9) microwave artificial chirality [20]; (10) “signal-processing�? metamaterials and “meta-machine�?, and (11) “digital�? metamaterials. 
 
(10) Nader Engheta earned Nader Engheta Masters and PhD degrees from the California Institute of Technology, and Nader Engheta B.S. degree from the school of engineering (Fanni) of the University of Tehran. 
(11) Professor Engheta moved to the United States of America in 1978 {{cn}}, and Nader Engheta and Nader Engheta family currently live in Philadelphia, Pennsylvania.

(3) A leading proponent of peaceful co-existence of democracy and Islam, Naser Khader established a new movement, Moderate Muslims (later renamed Democratic Muslims), when the Jyllands-Posten Muhammad cartoons controversy began. 
 
(4)In the national elections on 13 November 2007, Naser Khader's Liberal Alliance party won five parliamentary seats. 
(5) After a crumbling of the party and its membership base, Naser Khader himself left the party. 
(6) Following a short period as an Independent Member of the Danish Parliament, Naser Khader joined the Conservative People's Party on on March 17, 2009. 
(7) Khader lost Naser Khader mandate in the 2011 Danish parliamentary election.[1] 
 
(8)Khader is co-founder of an association of globally critically acclaimed Islamism critics, who work to promote freedom of speech and inspire moderate Muslims worldwide.[2] Khader, and the Conservative Party advocate a complete ban on the burqa as part of an integration initiative by the Conservatives' parliamentary group, describing it as un-Danish and oppression against women.[3]

(1){{Infobox golfer}} Orville James Moody (December 9, 1933 � August 8, 2008) was an American professional golfer who won numerous tournaments in Orville Moody career. 
(2) Orville Moody won the 1969 U.S. Open, the last champion in the 20th century to win through local and sectional qualifying.[1][2] 
 
(3)Moody was born in Chickasha, Oklahoma. 
(4) The son of a golf course superintendent, Orville Moody began Orville Moody career at Capitol Hill High School in Oklahoma City, winning the 1952 state high school golf championship. 
(5) After attempting college for a few weeks at the University of Oklahoma, Moody joined the U.S. Army. 
(6) Orville Moody was able to continue playing golf while in uniform, winning the All-Service championship and three Korea Opens. 
(7) Orville Moody spent 14 years in the Army, heading up maintenance supervision and instruction at all Army golf courses.[3] 
 
(8)Moody gave up Orville Moody military career in favor of a trial run at the PGA Tour in 1967. 
(9) Orville Moody nickname on the Tour was "Sarge" because Orville Moody rose to the rank of sergeant in the Army.[3] 
 
(10)Moody had limited success on the PGA Tour prior to 1969. 
(11) In April 1969, Orville Moody took part in a four-way playoff at the Greater Greensboro Open won by Gene Littler.[4] 
 
(12)The 1969 U.S. Open was played in June at the Cypress Creek Course of the Champions Golf Club in Houston, Texas. 
(13) Before the tournament began, defending champion Lee Trevino picked Moody to win. 
(14) Trevino saying "He's one helluva player."[5] 
Moody won by one stroke over Deane Beman, Al Geiberger and Bob Rosburg with a 72-hole score of 281.[6] 
(15)It was the only PGA Tour victory for Moody, who was also named PGA Player of the Year for 1969. 
(16) Orville Moody toured Japan, played in a few tournaments and eventually took a club pro job in Sulphur Springs, Texas.[3] 
(17) Moody was troubled by poor putting during Orville Moody early pro years. 
 
(18) Orville Moody luck on the Senior PGA Tour (now known as the Champions Tour) was dramatically different. 
(19) After turning 50, Orville Moody won three of Orville Moody first five tournaments and finished fifth on the money list on Orville Moody way to a total of 11 Senior PGA Tour victories. 
(20) In 1989, Orville Moody became only the fourth man to win both the U.S. Open and the U.S. Senior Open. 
(21) Moody went to a long putter after becoming a senior golfer, and this method improved Orville Moody putting significantly. 
 
(22)Moody had triple bypass heart surgery prior to the 1995 season, but still managed to play in 29 events. 
(23) Orville Moody died in 2008 from complications of multiple myeloma.

(1){{Use dmy dates}} {{Infobox Minister}} (1)Sir Patrick Gardiner Hastings KC (17 March 1880 – 26 February 1952) was a British barrister and politician noted for Patrick Hastings long and highly successful career as a barrister and Patrick Hastings short stint as Attorney General. 
(2) Patrick Hastings was educated at Charterhouse School until 1896, when Patrick Hastings family moved to continental Europe. 
(3) There Patrick Hastings learnt to shoot and ride horses, allowing him to join the Suffolk Imperial Yeomanry after the outbreak of the Second Boer War. 
(4) After demobilisation Patrick Hastings worked briefly as an apprentice to an engineer in Wales before moving to London to become a barrister. 
(5) Hastings joined the Middle Temple as a student on 4 November 1901, and after two years of saving money for the call to the Bar Patrick Hastings finally qualified as a barrister on 15 June 1904. 
 
(6)Hastings first rose to prominence as a result of the Case of the Hooded Man in 1912, and became noted for Patrick Hastings skill at cross-examinations. 
(7) After Patrick Hastings success in Gruban v Booth in 1917, Patrick Hastings practice steadily grew, and in 1919 Patrick Hastings became a King's Counsel (KC). 
(8) Following various successes as a KC in cases such as Sievier v Wootton and Russell v Russell, Patrick Hastings practice was put on hold in 1922 when Patrick Hastings was returned as the Labour Member of Parliament for Wallsend. 
(9) Hastings was appointed Attorney General for England and Wales in 1924, by the first Labour government, and knighted. 
(10) Patrick Hastings authorisation of the prosecution of J. R. Campbell in what became known as the Campbell Case, however, led to the fall of the government after less than a year in power. 
 
(11)Following Patrick Hastings resignation in 1926 to allow Margaret Bondfield to take a seat in Parliament, Hastings returned to Patrick Hastings work as a barrister, and was even more successful than before Patrick Hastings entry into the House of Commons. 
(12) Patrick Hastings cases included the Savidge Inquiry and the Royal Mail Case, and before Patrick Hastings full retirement in 1948 Patrick Hastings was one of the highest paid barristers at the English Bar. 
(13) As well as Patrick Hastings legal work, Hastings also tried Patrick Hastings hand at writing plays. 
(14) Although these had a mixed reception, The River was made into a silent film in 1927 named The Notorious Lady. 
(15) Following strokes in 1948 and 1949, Patrick Hastings activities became heavily restricted, and Patrick Hastings died at home on 26 February 1952.

(1){{Infobox astronaut}} (1)Peggy Annette Whitson (born February 9, 1960) is an American biochemistry researcher, NASA astronaut,[1] and NASA's Chief Astronaut. 
(2) Peggy Whitson first space mission was in 2002, with an extended stay aboard the International Space Station as a member of Expedition 5. Peggy Whitson second mission launched October 10, 2007, as the first female commander of the ISS with Expedition 16.[2][3] 
(3) With Peggy Whitson two long-duration stays abroad the ISS, Whitson is NASA's most experienced female astronaut, with just over 376 days in space.[4] 
(4) This also places Peggy Whitson twentieth among all space flyers. 
 
(5)The flight of Space Shuttle mission STS-120, commanded by female astronaut Pam Melroy, was the first time that two female mission commanders have been in orbit at the same time.[5][6][7] 
 
(6)On December 18, 2007, during the fourth spacewalk of Expedition 16 to inspect the S4 starboard Solar Alpha Rotary Joint (SARJ), the ground team in Mission Control informed Whitson that Peggy Whitson had become the female astronaut with the most cumulative EVA time in NASA history, as well as the most EVAs, with Peggy Whitson fifth EVA. 
(7) Three hours and 37 minutes into the spacewalk, Whitson surpassed NASA astronaut Sunita Williams with a total time at that point of 29 hours and 18 minutes.[8][9] 
(8) At the completion of Whitson's fifth EVA, the 100th in support of ISS assembly and maintenance, Whitson's cumulative EVA time became 32 hours, and 36 minutes, which placed Peggy Whitson in 20th place for total EVA time.[9] 
(9) Peggy Whitson sixth spacewalk, also during Expedition 16, brought Peggy Whitson cumulative EVA time to 39 hours, 46 minutes, which ranked Peggy Whitson 23rd for total EVA time as of November 2009.

(5) Brimelow is a paleoconservative.[3]

(7) Brimelow subsequently emigrated to Canada. 
(8) After a brief stint as a securities analyst, Peter Brimelow settled in Toronto, becoming a business writer and editor at the Financial Post and at Maclean's magazine. 
From 1978-80, Peter Brimelow was an aide to senator Orrin Hatch in Washington D.C.. 
(9)In 1980, Peter Brimelow moved to New York, working mainly for Barron's and Fortune. 
(10) Brimelow was senior editor of Forbes Magazine from 1986 to 2002. 
(11) Peter Brimelow was married to Maggy Laws Brimelow (1953�2004), a Canadian, until Peter Brimelow death following an eight-year battle with breast cancer. 
(12) Peter Brimelow and Peter Brimelow first wife had two children, a son (Alexander Brimelow) and a daughter (Hannah-Claire Brimelow). 
(13) Peter Brimelow married Lydia Sullivan, a 22-year old Heritage Foundation intern, in 2007; they had their first child, Felicity Brimelow in August 2010. 
(14) Their second child together, Karia Sybil Nancy Brimelow, was born on June 13, 2012. 
 
(15)In 1986, Brimelow published The Patriot Game: National Dreams and Political Realities, a book inspired partly by Goldwin Smith's Canada and the Canadian Question, published in 1891. 
(16) Brimelow's book helped galvanize the founding of the Reform Party of Canada in 1987 and inspired{{citation needed}} a number of individuals who now work in the government of Stephen Harper. 
 
(17)Brimelow's later books include the national best-seller Alien Nation: Common Sense About America's Immigration Disaster, The Wall Street Gurus: How You Can Profit from Investment Newsletters, and The Worm in the Apple: How the Teacher Unions Are Destroying American Education. 
(18) Alien Nation deals with immigration policy and the influx of illegal aliens as well as legal immigrants. 
(19) The Worm in the Apple discusses public education and teachers' unions, considering unions "highly destructive."[5] 
(20) Among views in The Worm in the Apple: "to attempt so far-reaching a goal as universal high school education is foolish."[6] 
(21) Ilana Mercer[7] and John O'Sullivan[8] praised the book. 
(22) For the Hoover Institution journal Education Next, public policy consultant George Mitchell wrote: "Brimelow...demonstrates how collective bargaining for teachers has produced labor agreements that stifle innovation and risk taking. 
(23) Peter Brimelow makes it clear that the dramatic rise in influence enjoyed by the teacher unions has coincided with stagnant and unacceptable levels of student performance." 
(24) However, in the same journal article, education consultant Julia E. Koppich took a more critical angle: "...Brimelow uses a variety of linguistic devices to drive home Peter Brimelow points. 
(25) But Peter Brimelow over-the-top language soon grates on the nerves...His argument is not that teacher unions are destroying American education, but that they labor long and hard to preserve the status quo...But this book contains so little about education-virtually nothing about classrooms, schools, or districts-even that point gets lost." 
(26) Koppich called the book "an anti-public school polemic."[9] 
 
(27) Peter Brimelow has appeared as a guest on The Political Cesspool, a pro-white talk-radio show. 
(28) Following the 2008 elections, Brimelow advocated that to win elections, the Republican Party should focus on "white votes".[10] 
(29) Peter Brimelow website VDARE has been rated by the Southern Poverty Law Center as a white nationalist hate group.[11][12] 
(30) Brimelow responded by applying what Peter Brimelow considered their loose standards of definition to themselves, saying "VDARE.COM has just been named a Hate Group by the Southern Poverty Law Center, the notorious Treason Group".[13] 
 
(31)Brimelow appeared on a panel discussing multiculturalism during the 2012 Conservative Political Action Conference (CPAC 2012), and gave a talk titled "The Failure of Multiculturalism: How the pursuit of diversity is weakening the American Identity." 
(32) During the panel discussion, Brimelow and other panelists said "immigration is polluting America." 
(33) In the face of condemnation from MSNBC and PFTAW, Al Cardenas of the American Conservative Union denied knowing him or Peter Brimelow reputation.[14]

(5) Philip Larkin was offered, but declined, the position of poet laureate in 1984, following the death of John Betjeman. 
 
(6)After graduating from Oxford in 1943 with a first in English language and literature, Larkin became a librarian. 
(7) It was during the thirty years Philip Larkin served as university librarian at the Brynmor Jones Library at the University of Hull that Philip Larkin produced the greater part of Philip Larkin published work. 
(8) Philip Larkin poems are marked by what Andrew Motion calls a very English, glum accuracy about emotions, places, and relationships, and what Donald Davie described as lowered sights and diminished expectations. 
(9) Eric Homberger called him "the saddest heart in the post-war supermarket"—Larkin himself said that deprivation for him was what daffodils were for Wordsworth.[3] 
(10) Influenced by W. H. Auden, W. B. Yeats, and Thomas Hardy, Philip Larkin poems are highly structured but flexible verse forms. 
(11) They were described by Jean Hartley, the ex-wife of Larkin's publisher George Hartley (The Marvell Press), as a "piquant mixture of lyricism and discontent",[4] though anthologist Keith Tuma writes that there is more to Larkin's work than its reputation for dour pessimism suggests.[5] 
 
(12)Larkin's public persona was that of the no-nonsense, solitary Englishman who disliked fame and had no patience for the trappings of the public literary life.[6] 
(13) The posthumous publication by Anthony Thwaite in 1992 of Philip Larkin letters triggered controversy about Philip Larkin personal life and political views, described by John Banville as hair-raising, but also in places hilarious.[6] 
(14) Lisa Jardine called him a "casual, habitual racist, and an easy misogynist", but the academic John Osborne argued in 2008 that "the worst that anyone has discovered about Larkin are some crass letters and a taste for porn softer than what passes for mainstream entertainment".[7] 
(15) Despite the controversy Larkin was chosen in a 2003 Poetry Book Society survey, almost two decades after Philip Larkin death, as Britain's best-loved poet of the previous 50 years, and in 2008 The Times named him Britain's greatest post-war writer.[8] 
 
(16)In 2010, 25 years after Philip Larkin death, Larkin's adopted home city, Kingston upon Hull commemorated him with the Larkin 25 Festival[9] which culminated in the unveiling of a statue of Larkin by Martin Jennings on 2 December 2010, the 25th anniversary of Philip Larkin death.[10][11][12]

(2) Piet de Jong served as Prime Minister of the Netherlands from April 5, 1967 until July 6, 1971.[1] 
 
(3)A veteran submarine commander of World War II. 
(4) De Jong graduated from the Royal Netherlands Naval College in 1934 and joined the Royal Netherlands Navy Submarine Service and served on Submarine O-24 during World War II, Piet de Jong ended the war as the commanding officer of that vessel, the Submarine O-24 was one of the few Dutch submarines that survived the war. 
(5) During Piet de Jong service in World War II Piet de Jong was awarded the Bronze Cross twice, the first time in 1934 and for the second time in 1940. 
Piet de Jong was also awarded the Distinguished Service Cross of the United Kingdom for Piet de Jong services during Second World War.[2] 
(6)After World War II De Jong continued to serve in the Royal Netherlands Navy, commanding two frigates and serving as aide-de-camp to Queen Juliana. 
(7) Piet de Jong eventually rose to the rank of Captain but retired from active service a year later when Piet de Jong was asked by the Catholic People's Party to serve on their behalf as State Secretary for Defence dealing with matters of the Royal Netherlands Navy. 
 
(8) Piet de Jong served as State Secretary for Defence from June 25, 1959 until July 24, 1963 in the Cabinet De Quay. 
(9) Piet de Jong later became Minister of Defence serving from July 24, 1963 until April 5, 1967 in the Cabinets Marijnen, Cals and Zijlstra. 
(10) After the Dutch general election of 1967, De Jong became Prime Minister of the Netherlands and Minister of General Affairs, leading the Cabinet de Jong. 
(11) Piet de Jong also served as acting Minister of Economic Affairs from January 7, 1970 until January 14, 1970 following the resignation of Leo de Block, Piet de Jong was succeeded by Roelof Nelissen. 
(12) The resignation of De Block marked the only change in the Council of Ministers during the full term of the cabinet. 
 
(13)Despite Piet de Jong popularity, the Catholic People's Party refused to nominate him as the lijsttrekker for the Dutch general election of 1971 because of Piet de Jong alleged "conservative image" and Piet de Jong was replaced by Piet de Jong Minister of Education and Science Gerard Veringa who had a more "progressive image". 
(14) After Piet de Jong premiership, De Jong remained in active politics and became the Parliamentary leader of the Catholic People's Party in the Senate and a Member of the Senate serving from May 11, 1971 until September 17, 1974 and finally retiring from active politics at the age of fifty nine. 
(15) Following the end of Piet de Jong active political career, De Jong occupied numerous seats on supervisory boards in the business and industry world.[3] 
 
(16)De Jong is known for Piet de Jong dry wit and Piet de Jong abilities as a team leader, Piet de Jong cabinet was the first cabinet after World War II that completed a full term without any internal conflicts. 
(17) As of 2012, Piet de Jong is the oldest and earliest serving former Prime Minister of the Netherlands and is one of the oldest living state leaders at the age of {{Age}}. 
(18) Over forty years after Piet de Jong left office, De Jong continues to comment on political affairs as a elder statesman.[4]

(2) Born in Victoria, Richard Minifie attended Melbourne Church of England Grammar School. 
(3) Travelling to the United Kingdom, Richard Minifie enlisted in the Royal Naval Air Service in June 1916. 
(4) Accepted for flight training, Richard Minifie completed Richard Minifie instruction in December and joined No.�1 (Naval) Squadron RNAS on the Western Front in January 1917, flying Sopwith Triplanes. 
(5) Richard Minifie went on to score seventeen aerial victories on this type of machine throughout the year, becoming both the youngest Australian flying ace of the First World War and No.�1 (Naval) Squadron's highest-scoring ace on the Triplane. 
(6) The unit re-equipped with the Sopwith Camel late in 1917, with Minifie going on to achieve a further four victories on the aircraft, raising Richard Minifie final tally to a score of twenty-one aircraft shot down. 
 
(7)Minifie crash landed in German-held territory in March 1918, and spent the remainder of the war in prisoner-of-war camps in Germany. 
(8) Richard Minifie was released at the end of the war, and was demobilised as a captain in September 1919. 
(9) Returning to Australia, Richard Minifie joined the staff of Richard Minifie father's flour milling business, James Minifie & Co. Pty Ltd. 
(10) Richard Minifie served as a squadron leader in the Air Training Corps of the Royal Australian Air Force during the Second World War. 
(11) Minifie returned to the flour milling industry after the war, becoming managing director of James Minifie & Co. Pty Ltd in 1949. 
(12) Richard Minifie died in 1969 at the age of seventy-one.

(1){{Infobox scientist}} Robert Charles Kennicutt, Jr. FRS is an American astronomer. 
(2) Robert Kennicutt is the Plumian Professor of Astronomy at the Institute of Astronomy in the University of Cambridge. 
(3) Robert Kennicutt was formerly Editor-in-Chief of the Astrophysical Journal (1999–2006). 
(4) Robert Kennicutt research interests include the structure and evolution of galaxies and star formation in galaxies. 
 
(5) Robert Kennicutt received Robert Kennicutt bachelor's degree in physics from Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute in 1973. 
(6) Robert Kennicutt was a graduate student in astronomy at the University of Washington, where Robert Kennicutt received Robert Kennicutt master's degree in 1976 and Robert Kennicutt Ph.D. in 1978. 
(7) Robert Kennicutt was awarded the Dannie Heineman Prize for Astrophysics in 2007 by the American Astronomical Society. 
(8) Robert Kennicutt shared the 2009 Gruber Prize in Cosmology with Wendy Freedman of the Carnegie Institution of Washington and Jeremy Mould of the University of Melbourne School of Physics, for their leadership in the definitive measurement of the value of the constant of proportionality in Hubble's Law. 
(9) Robert Kennicutt was made a fellow of the American Academy of Arts and Sciences in 2001 and appointed a Fellow of the Royal Society in 2011.

(1){{About}} (1){{Infobox person}} Robert R. Parrish (born 4 January 1916, Columbus, Georgia � 4 December 1995, Southampton, New York) was an American actor, film editor, film director, and writer. 
(2) Robert Parrish received an Academy Award for Film Editing for the 1947 film, Body and Soul. 
 
(3)Parrish was the son of factory cashier Gordon R. Parrish and Laura R. Parrish. 
(4) In the mid-1920s, the family moved from Georgia to Los Angeles and Parrish and Robert Parrish sisters Beverly and Helen began obtaining work as actors soon thereafter. 
(5) Parrish made Robert Parrish film debut in the 1927 Our Gang short Olympic Games. 
(6) (Their mother, Laura R. Parrish, was an actress as well and appeared in a few films of the 1940s.) 
(7) Robert Parrish appeared in the anti-war classic All Quiet on the Western Front (1930) and Charles Chaplin's City Lights (1931), and in several films for John Ford. 
 
(8)Ford then enlisted him as an assistant editor in 1936 on Mary of Scotland, and as a sound editor on Young Mr Lincoln (1939). 
(9) Parrish worked as an assistant editor and sound editor on other Ford movies as Drums Along the Mohawk (1939) and The Grapes of Wrath (1940). 
(10) Parrish and Ford were in the United States Navy during the Second World War, and worked on documentary and training films including The Battle of Midway (1942). 
 
(11)In 1947 Robert Parrish won an Oscar for Robert Parrish debut as a feature film editor on Robert Rossen's high tempo boxing drama Body and Soul; the award was shared with Francis Lyon. 
(12) Parrish was later nominated for another Rossen film – the political drama All the King’s Men (1949); Robert Parrish shared the nomination with Al Clark. 
 
(13)Parrish went on to contribute Robert Parrish technical talents to a host of highly regarded films and made a promising directorial debut in 1951 with the gripping revenge melodrama, Cry Danger. 
(14) Robert Parrish subsequent output met with varying success. 
(15) The Purple Plain (1954) was nominated for "Best British film" at the 8th British Academy Film Awards. 
(16) One of the most notorious of Robert Parrish films was the James Bond Parody Casino Royale (1967), in which Robert Parrish was one of the film's five directors. 
(17) Robert Parrish last film, on which Robert Parrish shared co-director credit with Bertrand Tavernier, was Mississippi Blues (1983). 
 
(18)Parrish wrote two memoirs, Growing Up in Hollywood (1976) and its sequel Hollywood Doesn't Live Here Anymore (1988). 
(19) Of the first, Kevin Brownlow wrote, "His stories about these pictures were marvellous in themselves, and Robert Parrish often came at them sideways, so not only the punchline but the situation took you by surprise. 
(20) We all entreated him to write them down and in 1976 Robert Parrish did so, producing one of the most enchanting - and hilarious - books about the picture business ever written. 
(21) It was called Growing Up in Hollywood and it ought to be reprinted in this centenary year."[1] 
(22) Summing up Parrish's career, Allen Grant Richards wrote, "Other than Robert Parrish excellent editing work and early directing, Parrish may be most remembered as storyteller from Robert Parrish two books of Hollywood memoirs."[2]

(2) By 1963, Rubel Phillips had switched parties to become only the third Republican since 1877 to seek Rubel Phillips state's governorship. 
(3) Phillips ran on the slogan of "K.O. the Kennedys",[3] even though Rubel Phillips had backed U.S. Senator John F. Kennedy for the presidency in 1960 over the Republican Vice President Richard M. Nixon. 
(4) Phillips, with 38 percent of the ballots cast, lost to Democrat Paul B. Johnson, Jr., the son of a former governor. 
(5) That election was held barely two weeks prior to the assassination of President Kennedy in Dallas, Texas. 
 
(6)In 1967, Phillips ran as a moderate Republican but lost decisively to the conservative Democrat John Bell Williams, a U.S. representative.

Russ Courtnall played for the Toronto Maple Leafs, Montreal Canadiens, Minnesota North Stars, Dallas Stars, Vancouver Canucks, New York Rangers and Los Angeles Kings 
(2)Coming out of junior hockey, Russ Courtnall was considered one of the top prospects in 1983 and the Toronto Maple Leafs selected him seventh overall in the Entry Draft 
(2)In 1982–83, Courtnall scored 36 goals and 61 assists for 97 points in 60 games. 
(3) After being drafted by the Leafs, Courtnall appeared in just 32 games with the Cougars, scoring 29 goals and 66 points before being called up to the Maple Leafs, who were in dire need of scoring help at the time. 
(4) As a rookie with Toronto, Russ Courtnall scored 12 goals and 22 points in 69 games. 
(5) Although Russ Courtnall goal production was not as high as the Leafs had hoped for, Russ Courtnall speed opened up opportunities, especially for Russ Courtnall linemates. 
 
(6)In 1984, Courtnall also suited up for the Canadian Olympic team, which finished out of the medals at the Sarajevo Games. 
(7) Upon returning, Russ Courtnall rejoined the Maple Leafs. 
(8) In 1985–86,(playing alongside fellow Notre Dame Hounds alumni Gary Leeman and rookie Wendel Clark on what was dubbed The Hound Line) Courtnall broke the 20-goal barrier, notching 22 to go along with 38 assists for 60 points. 
(9) Russ Courtnall offensive numbers improved the following year with 29 goals and 73 points. 
 
(10)In trying to force Courtnall to play a tougher brand of hockey, the club saw Russ Courtnall offensive production decrease in 1987–88. 
(11) After just nine games in 1989–89, the Maple Leafs dealt Courtnall to the Montreal Canadiens for enforcer John Kordic. 
(12) Although Courtnall's stock had clearly gone down in the eyes of Maple Leafs' management, most observers felt Montreal got a steal in that trade. 
 
(13)In 64 games, Courtnall put up 39 points with the Habs, but Russ Courtnall fast skating and stickhandling abilities made him a fan favourite. 
(14) Courtnall saved Russ Courtnall best performance for the playoffs, where the Canadiens advanced all the way to the Stanley Cup finals before losing to the Calgary Flames. 
(15) In 21 post-season games, Courtnall contributed eight goals and 13 points. 
 
(16)Courtnall enjoyed another two-and-a-half years in Montreal before joining the Minnesota North Stars for the 1992–93 season. 
(17) In 84 games, Russ Courtnall managed a career-high 36 goals and 43 assists for 79 points. 
(18) Russ Courtnall followed that up with an 80-point season in the team's first year of play in Dallas. 
(19) Late in the 1994–95 season, Courtnall was traded to the Vancouver Canucks where Russ Courtnall teamed with Russ Courtnall brother, Geoff Courtnall, for 13 games that year. 
 
(20)In 1997–98, Courtnall signed as a free agent with the Los Angeles Kings, where Russ Courtnall played until Russ Courtnall retirement following the 1998–99 season. 
(21) Russ Courtnall finished with 297 goals, 447 assists and 744 points in a 15-year NHL career. 
 
(22)Courtnall was a participant in both the second and third seasons of Battle of the Blades, the latter as a last-minute replacement for the late Wade Belak, but was eliminated early in the competition on both occasions.
