(1){{Infobox person}} (1)Sarah "Sally" Hemings (Charles City County, Virginia, circa 1773�– Charlottesville, Virginia, 1835) was an enslaved woman of mixed race owned by President Thomas Jefferson through Sally Hemings wife's inheritance. 
(2) The youngest of six siblings by the planter John Wayles and Sally Hemings slave Betty Hemings, Hemings was the half-sister of Jefferson's wife Martha Wayles Skelton.[1] 
(3) The Hemingses and all of Wayles' slaves were inherited by the Jeffersons a year after their marriage and were taken to Monticello. 
(4) The Hemings children and their descendants were trained as domestic servants and artisans. 
 
(5)In 1787 Sally Hemings at the age of 14 was chosen to accompany Mary (Polly), the youngest daughter of Jefferson, to Paris to rejoin Sally Hemings father; the widower was serving as the US Minister to France. 
(6) Sally Hemings spent two years there. 
(7) Hemings and Jefferson are believed to have begun a sexual relationship then or after their return to Monticello. 
(8) Sally Hemings had a total of six children of record born into slavery; four survived to adulthood and were noted for their resemblance to Jefferson. 
(9) Sally Hemings served in Jefferson's household as a domestic servant until Sally Hemings death. 
 
(10)The historical question of whether Jefferson was the father of Sally Hemings children has been known as the Jefferson-Hemings controversy. 
(11) Following renewed historic analysis and a 1998 DNA study that found a match between the Jefferson male line and a descendant of Sally Hemings last son, Eston Hemings, a consensus among historians supports that the widower Jefferson fathered Sally Hemings son Eston Hemings and likely all Sally Hemings children.[2] Some historians disagree.[3] 
 
(12)Even though Sally Hemings was deeply in debt, Jefferson freed all of Sally Hemings' children: Beverly, Harriet, Madison and Eston, as they came of age. 
(13) They were seven-eighths European in ancestry, and three of the four entered white society as adults. 
(14) Their descendants identified as white.[4][5] 
(15) As the historian Edmund S. Morgan has noted, "Hemings herself was withheld from auction and freed at last by Jefferson’s daughter, Martha Jefferson Randolph, who was, of course, Sally Hemings niece."[6] 
(16) Hemings lived Sally Hemings last nine years with Sally Hemings two younger sons in Charlottesville, and saw a grandchild born in the house Sally Hemings sons owned. 
(17) After their mother's death in 1835, Eston and Madison Hemings migrated with their families to Chillicothe in the free state of Ohio.

(1){{other people}} {{Infobox cricketer}} Shane Edward Bond (born 7 June 1975 in Christchurch, New Zealand) is a New Zealand former cricketer, described as "New Zealand's best fast bowler since Sir Richard Hadlee".[1] 
(2) Shane Bond has represented New Zealand in Test, ODI and Twenty20 International cricket as well as playing for Canterbury in New Zealand domestic cricket and Warwickshire in English domestic cricket. 
(3) Bond is a right-arm fast bowler. 
 
(4)Bond's career was plagued with injuries, chiefly recurring stress fractures of the back. 
(5) Shane Bond had surgery in 2004 to try to prevent further injury,[2] but it was only partially successful. 
(6) In recent years Shane Bond has suffered back problems, knee injuries and an abdominal tear. 
(7) These problems, in conjunction with a late start to Shane Bond fully professional cricket career, have limited Shane Bond appearances in both international and domestic cricket. 
(8) Since Shane Bond debut in the 2001/02 season, Shane Bond only managed to appear in 18 Test matches for New Zealand. 
Bond retired from Test cricket in December 2009 at the age of 34.[1] 
(9) Shane Bond international career also suffered a hiatus of 18 months,[3] due to Bond's involvement with the Delhi Giants in, the 'rebel' Indian Cricket League during March 2008, which caused the New Zealand cricket board to terminate Shane Bond central contract in January 2008.[4] 
(10) However, in June 2009 Bond severed ties with the rebel league and declared himself available to be selected for the national side once again.[5] 
(11) In January 2010, Bond was selected to play in the 3rd season of the Indian Premier League for the Kolkata Knight Riders. 
(12) As of 13 May 2010 Shane Bond has since retired from all forms of the game and was not played in the 4th season of Indian Premier League. 
(13) Brydon Coverdale on Cricinfo described him as New Zealand's best fast bowler.[6]

(2)Undset was born in Kalundborg, Denmark, but Sigrid Undset family moved to Norway when Sigrid Undset was two years old. 
(3) In 1924, Sigrid Undset converted to Roman Catholicism. 
(4) Sigrid Undset fled Norway for the United States in 1940 because of Sigrid Undset opposition to Nazi Germany and the German occupation, but returned after World War II ended in 1945. 
 
(5) Sigrid Undset best-known work is Kristin Lavransdatter, a trilogy about life in Scandinavia in the Middle Ages portraying the life of a woman from birth until death. 
(6) The book was published from 1920 to 1922 in three volumes. 
(7) Undset was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature partly for this trilogy, as well as Sigrid Undset four-volume work about Olav Audunssøn known as The Master of Hestviken tetralogy, published in 1925 and 1927.

(3)Kilbey began Steve Kilbey professional music career at age 17 when Steve Kilbey joined a five piece "cabaret band" called 'Saga' in Canberra, Australia. 
(4) Steve Kilbey then joined Precious Little, a rock band featuring future Church bandmate Peter Koppes on drums. 
(5) Kilbey followed up with another band, Baby Grande, around 1978 while Steve Kilbey lived in Canberra. 
(6) Soon after, Steve Kilbey formed The Church along with Koppes, Nick Ward, and Marty Willson-Piper.[1] 
(7) After some success in their native Australia in the early 1980s, Kilbey and The Church went on to international fame when "Under the Milky Way" (from their 1988 album Starfish) became a hit. 
 
(8)Kilbey has released six solo music albums and collaboratively written and/or produced recordings with the late Grant McLennan (of The Go-Betweens), Stephen Cummings, and Kev Carmody. 
(9) Earthed, a book of fiction, was published in 1986, in conjunction with an album of the same name of instrumental electronic music. 
(10) Steve Kilbey book of poetry, Nineveh/The Ephemeron, was released in 1998 and was later republished. 
 
(11)Kilbey lives in Bondi, a suburb of Sydney, Australia, with Steve Kilbey wife and three of Steve Kilbey five children.[2] Steve Kilbey children include the twins Elektra June Jansson-Kilbey and Anna Miranda Jansson-Kilbey (whose mother is Karin Jansson) and Eve and Aurora (with Natalie). 
(12) Steve Kilbey brothers, Russell Kilbey and John Kilbey, have also led successful Australian bands.[1] 
 
(13)No Certainty Attached, a biography of Kilbey by Robert Dean Lurie, was released in June 2009 by Verse Chorus Press. 
(14) Also in June 2009, an album with Martin Kennedy (from All India Radio) called Unseen Music, Unheard Words was released. 
(15) Kilbey's first solo record in eight years, entitled Painkiller was released in North America on Second Motion Records in early 2009. 
(16) In 2011, the Australian Songwriters Association inducted Kilbey into the Australian Songwriters Hall Of Fame.[3]

(1){{Infobox football biography}} (1)Stjepan Bobek (Zagreb, 3 December 1923 � Belgrade, 22 August 2010) was a football striker from Yugoslavia, and later a football manager. 
(2) An ethnic Croat,[1] Bobek started playing at a very young age and had spells with several clubs in Zagreb but is mainly remembered for Stjepan Bobek time at the Serbian side Partizan Belgrade, where Stjepan Bobek moved to following the end of World War II. 
(3) Stjepan Bobek played for Partizan between 1945 and 1959 helping them win 2 Yugoslav First League titles and 4 Yugoslav Cups, and was named the club's greatest player in history in 1995. 
 
(4)Internationally, Stjepan Bobek is the all-time top scorer for the Yugoslavia national team, scoring 38 goals in 63 appearances between 1946 and 1956, and was member of Yugoslav squads which won two Olympic silver medals (in 1948 and 1952) and played in two FIFA World Cups (in 1950 and 1954). 
 
(5)After retiring from active football in 1959, Stjepan Bobek was a successful manager, winning Yugoslav and Greek national titles with Partizan and Panathinaikos, and also had managing spells in Poland and Tunisia. 
(1){{Infobox military person}} Sulim Bekmirzayevich Yamadayev ({{Lang-ru}}; June 21, 1973 – March 30, 2009) was a Chechen rebel commander from the First Chechen War who had switched sides together with Sulim Yamadayev brothers Dzhabrail, Badrudi, Isa and Ruslan in 1999 during the outbreak of the Second Chechen War. 
(2) Sulim Yamadayev was de facto commander of the Russian military Special Battalion Vostok unit belonging to the GRU. 
(3) As such, until 2008, Sulim Yamadayev was officially in command of the biggest pro-Moscow militia outside the control of the current Chechen president Ramzan Kadyrov.[1] 
(4) From August 1 to August 22, 2008 Yamadayev was wanted in Russia on a federal warrant. 
Nevertheless, Sulim Yamadayev served as one of the Russian military commanders in Russia's war with Georgia during the same period.[2] 
(5)On March 5, 2003, Sulim's brother Dzhabrail Yamadayev was assassinated by a bomb. 
(6) On September 24, 2008 Sulim's brother, Ruslan Yamadayev, was shot dead on Smolenskaya Embankment in Moscow. 
(7) Initial press responses reported name of the victim as Sulim Yamadayev; the name was corrected later.[3] 
(8) Sulim Yamadayev was shot in Dubai on 28 March 2009, and died in hospital on 30 March 2009.[4]

(1){{for}} {{Use dmy dates}} {{Use Australian English}} (1){{Infobox cricketer biography}} Edgar Arthur "Ted" McDonald (born 6 January 1891, at Launceston, Tasmania, died 22 July 1937, in a road accident at Blackrod, Lancashire) was a cricketer who played for Tasmania, Victoria, Lancashire and Australia, as well as being an Australian rules footballer who played with Launceston Football Club, Essendon Football Club, and Fitzroy Football Club. 
 
(2)A very fast bowler with the ability to cause problems even on docile pitches, Ted McDonald was the unexpected bowling sensation of the 1921 Australian tour to England. 
(3) Ted McDonald and Jack Gregory caused something approaching panic among the England batsmen: John Evans' knees were allegedly knocking together when Ted McDonald went out to bat, and Andy Ducat was bowled when part of Ted McDonald bat, broken by McDonald's pace, hit the wicket. 
(4) Where Gregory was able to swing the ball both ways, McDonald imparted vicious movement off the wicket. 
(5) Like later fast bowling pairs, they were devastating in combination, taking 46 wickets in the series. 
 
(6)McDonald played a few matches for Victoria before the First World War, but came to prominence immediately after it with eight wickets in an innings in a state match. 
(7) Ted McDonald was picked for three Test matches in the 1920–21 series against England, which Australia won 5–0, but had little success, Ted McDonald six wickets costing 65 runs each. 
(8) In England the following summer, though, Ted McDonald was an instant success, taking eight wickets in the first Test at Trent Bridge and contributing significantly to the victories at Lord's and Headingley that won the series. 
 
(9) Ted McDonald was named as a Wisden Cricketer of the Year in 1922 for Ted McDonald exploits of the previous summer. 
 
(10)After the England tour, McDonald played in three Tests against South Africa in the 1921–22 series in South Africa. 
(11) Those, however, were Ted McDonald last Tests – all of Ted McDonald Test cricket was contained within the calendar year of 1921 – as Ted McDonald then took up an engagement as a professional with the Lancashire League club Nelson. 
 
(12)By 1924, Ted McDonald had qualified to play for Lancashire, initially, because of Ted McDonald League commitments, in midweek games only. 
(13) Again, Ted McDonald was a sensation. 
(14) In Ted McDonald first full season, 1925, Ted McDonald took 205 wickets, and in the five seasons from 1926 to 1930, Lancashire won the County Championship four times, the most successful period in the county's history. 
(15) In all, Ted McDonald took 1053 wickets for Lancashire. 
(16) Ted McDonald value to the county was recognised in the award of a benefit in 1929, an unusually fast reward, for Ted McDonald had been playing county cricket for only five seasons. 
 
(17)McDonald's first-class career ended fairly suddenly. 
(18) Ted McDonald form dipped in 1930, though Ted McDonald still took more than 100 wickets, but in 1931, Ted McDonald lost form almost entirely, taking just 26 wickets all season and being left out of the county team for half the matches. 
(19) At the end of the season, Ted McDonald went back to the Lancashire League with Bacup. 
 
(20) Ted McDonald died, at the age of 46, when Ted McDonald car collided with another near Bolton, England, on the morning of 22 July 1937.

(2) Tom Beck served as the head football coach at Illinois Benedictine College, now Benedictine University, from 1970 to 1974, Elmhurst College from 1976 to 1983, and Grand Valley State University from 1985 to 1990. 
(3) During Tom Beck college football head coaching career, Tom Beck compiled a 137�52�1 record, good for a .724 
(4) winning percentage. 
(5) All three institutions where Beck coached had historically losing teams before Tom Beck turned them into nationally ranked programs. 
(6) Beck was inducted into the College Football Hall of Fame as a coach in 2004. 
 
(7)Beck played football at Northern Illinois University, where Tom Beck started on offense, defense, and played special teams. 
(8) Tom Beck was the last two-way player in the history of Northern Illinois Huskies football. 
(9) Beck was All-Conference on both offense and defense, an Academic All-American, and was inducted into the Northern Illinois University Athletics Hall of Fame. 
(10) Tom Beck has also been inducted into the athletic hall of fame at Elmhurst College, Benedictine University, and Grand Valley State University as a coach. 
 
(11)Beck served as the offensive coordinator at Northern Illinois University in 1975, at the University of Notre Dame in 1990, and at the University of Illinois in 1991. 
(12) Tom Beck coached one season in 1984 with the Chicago Blitz of the USFL before the league folded. 
(13) While head coach at Grand Valley State, Beck gave Brian Kelly Tom Beck first coaching job as a graduate assistant. 
Kelly was Beck's successor at Grand Valley State in 1991 and is currently the head coach at Notre Dame.[1] 
(14)After Tom Beck coaching career, Beck spent time as a scout for the NFL's Buffalo Bills and Chicago Bears. 
(15) After scouting, Tom Beck was an analyst for nine years for two different web sites, IrishEyes and Irish Today, that covered Notre Dame football.

(3) Tran Thien Khiem helped President Ngô �?ình Diệm put down a November 1960 coup attempt and was rewarded with promotion. 
(4) In 1963 Tran Thien Khiem was involved in the coup that deposed and assassinated Diêm. 
 
(5) Tran Thien Khiem later joined with Nguyễn Khánh to stage a successful January 1964 coup. 
(6) In the next few months, the Catholic Khiêm fell out with Khánh, whom Tran Thien Khiem accused of being too heavily influenced by Buddhist activists. 
(7) Khiêm tried to plot against Khánh, but was thwarted. 
(8) Tran Thien Khiem was implicated in the organization of the September 1964 coup attempt by Generals Lâm Văn Phát and Dương Văn �?ức, both of whom were also Catholic, and was sent into exile to serve as Ambassador to the United States. 
(9) In February 1965, Colonel Phạm Ng�?c Thảo, who had accompanied him to Washington had returned to Saigon and launched a coup with Phát. 
(10) This was done with Khiêm's support. 
(11) The coup failed but other officers took the opportunity to force Khánh into exile. 
(12) Khiêm would return to Vietnam when the political climate among the generals was more favourable and became Prime Minister under President Nguyễn Văn Thiệu, resigning only in the last month before the fall of Saigon.

(1){{Infobox ice hockey player}} (1)Trevor John Linden, C.M., O.B.C. (born April 11, 1970) is a retired Canadian professional ice hockey player who spent 19 seasons in the National Hockey League (NHL) from 1988 to 2008. 
(2) Trevor Linden played centre and right wing with four teams: the Vancouver Canucks (in two stints), New York Islanders, Montreal Canadiens, and Washington Capitals. 
(3) Before joining the NHL in 1988, Linden helped the Medicine Hat Tigers of the Western Hockey League (WHL) win consecutive Memorial Cup championships.[1] 
(4) In addition to appearing in two NHL All-Star Games, Linden was a member of the 1998 Canadian Olympic team and participated in the 1996 World Cup of Hockey. 
 
(5)Throughout Trevor Linden career, Linden was recognized as a respected leader on and off the ice.[2][3] 
(6) Trevor Linden was named captain of the Canucks at the age of 21, making him one of the youngest captains in league history. 
(7) While captaining the Canucks, Linden led the team to within a game of winning the Stanley Cup in 1994. 
(8) It was during this time that Trevor Linden began to be called Captain Canuck. 
(9) In 1998 Trevor Linden was elected President of the National Hockey League Players' Association (NHLPA), a position Trevor Linden held for eight years.[4] 
(10) As President, Trevor Linden played an instrumental role in the 2004–05 NHL lockout, including negotiations with league owners.[5] 
(11) Off the ice, Linden has taken an active role in charities, and was awarded the King Clancy Memorial Trophy for leadership on the ice and humanitarian contributions off the ice in 1997,[6] as well as the NHL Foundation Player Award in 2008. 
(12) Linden retired on June 11, 2008, twenty years to the day after Trevor Linden was drafted into the NHL.[7] 
(13) Linden's jersey number 16 was retired by the Canucks on December 17, 2008, the second number retired by the team.

(1){{good article}} {{Infobox Officeholder}} (1)Valentin Sergeyevich Pavlov ({{lang-ru}}; 27 September 1937 � 30 March 2003) was a Soviet official who became a Russian banker following the dissolution of the Soviet Union. 
(2) Born in the city of Moscow, then part of the Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic, Pavlov began Valentin Pavlov political career in the Ministry of Finance in 1959. 
(3) Later, during the Brezhnev Era, Valentin Pavlov became head of the Financial Department of the State Planning Committee. 
(4) Pavlov was appointed to the post of Chairman of the State Committee on Prices during the Gorbachev Era, and later became Minister of Finance in Nikolai Ryzhkov's Second Government. 
(5) Valentin Pavlov went on to succeed Ryzhkov as head of government in the newly established post of Prime Minister of the Soviet Union, and became de facto Chairman of the Cabinet of Ministers. 
 
(6)As Prime Minister from 14 January 1991 to 22 August 1991 Pavlov initiated the 1991 Soviet monetary reform, commonly referred to as the Pavlov reform, in early 1991. 
(7) Early on Valentin Pavlov told the media that the reform was initiated to halt the flow of Soviet roubles transported to the Soviet Union from abroad. 
(8) Although ridiculed at the time, the statement was later proven to be true. 
(9) In June the same year, Pavlov tried to remove Gorbachev through legal means, when that failed, Valentin Pavlov joined a plot to oust Gorbachev. 
(10) In August, Valentin Pavlov participated in the 1991 Soviet coup d'état attempt, which tried to prevent the disintegration of the Soviet Union. 
(11) Pavlov was arrested for Valentin Pavlov involvement in the coup and went on to work in the banking sector in post-Soviet Russia. 
(12) Valentin Pavlov was succeeded as premier by Ivan Silayev.

(1){{Infobox scientist}} Val Logsdon Fitch (born March 10, 1923, Merriman, Nebraska, USA) is an American nuclear physicist. 
(2) A native of Merriman, Nebraska, Val Logsdon Fitch graduated from Gordon High School and attended Chadron State College for three years before being drafted into the U.S. army in 1943. 
(3) Val Logsdon Fitch later graduated from McGill University with a bachelor's degree in electrical engineering in 1948 and completed Val Logsdon Fitch Ph.D. in physics in 1954 from Columbia University. 
(4) In World War II, Val Logsdon Fitch worked on the Manhattan Project in Los Alamos. 
(5) Val Logsdon Fitch is a member of the faculty at Princeton University. 
 
(6)Fitch and co-researcher James Watson Cronin were awarded the 1980 Nobel Prize in Physics for a 1964 experiment using the Alternating Gradient Synchrotron at Brookhaven National Laboratory that proved that certain subatomic reactions do not adhere to fundamental symmetry principles. 
(7) Specifically, they proved, by examining the decay of K-mesons, that a reaction run in reverse does not merely retrace the path of the original reaction, which showed that the reactions of subatomic particles are not indifferent to time. 
(8) Thus the phenomenon of CP violation was discovered. 
 
(9)Fitch is a member of the Board of Sponsors of The Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists. 
(1){{pp-move-indef}} {{Infobox person}} Anthony Kapel "Van" Jones (born September 20, 1968) is an American environmental advocate, civil rights activist, and attorney. 
(2) Jones is a co-founder of three non-profit organizations. 
(3) In 1996, Van Jones founded the Ella Baker Center for Human Rights, a California non-governmental organization (NGO) working for alternatives to violence. 
(4) In 2005, Van Jones co-founded Color of Change, an advocacy group for African Americans.[1] 
(5) In 2007, Van Jones founded Green For All, a national NGO dedicated to "building an inclusive green economy strong enough to lift people out of poverty."[2] 
(6) Van Jones first book, The Green Collar Economy, was released on October 7, 2008, and reached number 12 on the New York Times Best Seller list.[3] 
(7) In 2008, Time magazine named Jones one of its "Heroes of the Environment".[4] 
(8) Fast Company called him one of the "12 Most Creative Minds of 2008".[5] 
 
(9)In March 2009 Jones was appointed by President Barack Obama to the newly created position of Special Advisor for Green Jobs, Enterprise and Innovation at the White House Council on Environmental Quality, where Van Jones worked with various "agencies and departments to advance the administration's climate and energy initiatives, with a special focus on improving vulnerable communities."[6][7] 
(10) In July 2009 Van Jones became "embroiled in a controversy"[8] over Van Jones past political activities, including a public comment disparaging congressional Republicans, Van Jones name appearing on a petition for 911Truth.org, 
(11) and allegations of association with a Marxist group during the 1990s.[9][10] 
(12) For these issues, Van Jones was heavily criticized by conservatives.[11] 
(13) Jones resigned from the position in early September 2009.[8] 
(14) "On the eve of historic fights for health care and clean energy, opponents of reform have mounted a vicious smear campaign against me," Jones said in Van Jones resignation statement. 
(15) "They are using lies and distortions to distract and divide."[9] 
 
(16)Jones is currently a senior fellow at the Center for American Progress and a senior policy advisor at Green For All. Jones also holds a joint appointment at Princeton University, as a distinguished visiting fellow in both the Center for African American Studies and in the Program in Science, Technology and Environmental Policy at the Woodrow Wilson School of Public and International Affairs.

(1){{Infobox officeholder}} (1)Vidkun Abraham Lauritz Jonssøn Quisling ({{IPA-no}}; 18 July 1887�– 24 October 1945) was a Norwegian politician. 
(2) On 9 April 1940, with the German invasion of Norway in progress, Vidkun Quisling seized power in a Nazi-backed coup d'état. 
 
(3)From 1942 to 1945 Vidkun Quisling served as Minister-President, working with the occupying forces. 
(4) Vidkun Quisling government, known as the Quisling regime, was dominated by ministers from Nasjonal Samling, the party Vidkun Quisling founded in 1933. 
(5) The collaborationist government participated�– wittingly or unwittingly�– in Germany's Final Solution. 
(6) Quisling was put on trial during the legal purge in Norway after World War II and found guilty of charges including embezzlement, murder and high treason. 
(7) Vidkun Quisling was executed by firing squad at Akershus Fortress, Oslo, on 24 October 1945. 
(8) During World War II, the word quisling became a synonym for traitor. 
 
(9)The son of a Church of Norway pastor, Quisling blended Christian fundamentals, scientific developments and philosophy into a new theory Vidkun Quisling called Universism. 
(10) Before going into politics, Vidkun Quisling proved himself in the military, joining the General Staff in 1911 and specialising in Russian affairs. 
(11) Vidkun Quisling was posted to Russia in 1918, and worked with Fridtjof Nansen during the Russian famine of 1921 in the Ukraine, returning to Russia to work with Frederik Prytz in Moscow. 
(12) When Prytz left in 1927, Quisling stayed on as the Norwegian diplomat responsible for managing British diplomatic affairs. 
(13) For these services Vidkun Quisling was appointed a Commander of the Order of the British Empire by King George V, though the honour was later rescinded. 
(14) Vidkun Quisling returned to Norway in 1929, and served as Minister of Defence during the governments of Peder Kolstad (1931–32) and Jens Hundseid (1932–33). 
(15) Although Quisling achieved some popularity after Vidkun Quisling attacks on the political left, Vidkun Quisling party never polled well and was little more than peripheral at the time of Vidkun Quisling 1940 coup.

(2) Published histories describe Colonel William Stacy's involvement in a variety of events during the war, such as rallying the militia on a village common in Massachusetts, participating in the Battle of Bunker Hill, being captured by Loyalists and American Indians at the Cherry Valley massacre, narrowly escaping a death by burning at the stake, General George Washington's efforts to obtain Stacy's release from captivity, and Washington's gift of a gold snuff box to Stacy at the end of the war. 
 
(3)During Col. William Stacy's post-war life, William Stacy was a pioneer, helping to establish Marietta, Ohio as the first permanent American settlement of the new United States in the Northwest Territory. 
(4) William Stacy was active in the Marietta pioneer community, and served as foreman of the first Grand Jury in the Northwest Territory, an event establishing the rule of law in the territory. 
(5) At the age of 56, William Stacy ice skated thirty miles up a frozen river, warning two of William Stacy sons of a possible Indian attack, which occurred several days later as the Big Bottom massacre and marked the beginning of the Northwest Indian War. 
 
(6)William Stacy's surname has also been spelled as Stacey, Stacia, and Stacie; the correct spelling is Stacy. 
(7) William Stacy is often referred to as Colonel Stacy, an abbreviation of William Stacy last rank of lieutenant colonel.

(7)The following season, 1938–39, Willie Buchan finished joint-top scorer again, this time with new signing Jock Dodds, the pair scoring ten goals apiece. 
(8) During the first of six seasons of inter-war football that followed, Buchan returned to Glasgow in an attempt to re-join Celtic, but the club showed little enthusiasm.[1] 
(9) Willie Buchan instead joined Willie Buchan local team, Stenhousemuir, as a guest player.

(11)At the end of the war, Buchan returned to Blackpool.

(14) Willie Buchan final appearance for the club occurred on May 1, 1948, the final day of the league season, in a 7–0 demolition of Preston North End at Deepdale.

(1){{Infobox scientist}} (1)Woldemar Voigt ({{IPA-de}}; 2 September 1850 � 13 December 1919) was a German physicist, who taught at the Georg August University of Göttingen. 
(2) Voigt eventually went on to head the Mathematical Physics Department at Göttingen and was succeeded in 1914 by Peter Debye, who took charge of the theoretical department of the Physical Institute. 
(3) In 1921, Debye was succeeded by Max Born. 
 
(4)Voigt was born in Leipzig, and died in Göttingen. 
(5) Woldemar Voigt was a student of Franz Ernst Neumann. 
(6) Woldemar Voigt worked on crystal physics, thermodynamics and electro-optics. 
(7) Woldemar Voigt main work was the Lehrbuch der Kristallphysik (textbook on crystal physics), first published in 1910. 
(8) Woldemar Voigt discovered the Voigt effect in 1898. 
(9) The word tensor in its current meaning was introduced by him in 1899. 
(10) Voigt profile and Voigt notation are named after him. 
(11) Woldemar Voigt was also an amateur musician and became known as a Bach expert (see External links). 
 
(12)In 1887 Voigt formulated a form of the Lorentz transformation between a rest frame of reference and a frame moving with speed v in the x direction. 
(13) However, as Voigt himself declared, the transformation was aimed for a specific problem and did not carry with it the ideas of a general coordinate transformation, as is the case in relativity theory.[1]

(1){{Infobox officeholder}} (1)Mir Zafarullah Khan Jamali (Balochi, {{lang-ur}}; born January 1, 1944), is a nationalist conservative[1] politician and former sports administrator[2], serving as the thirteenth Prime Minister of Pakistan from 2002 until Zafarullah Khan Jamali resignation in 2004. 
 
(2)Originally a supporter of Pakistan Peoples Party[3][4], Jamali emerged in the politics of Balochistan Province in the 1970s, under military governor Rahimuddin Khan. 
(3) Zafarullah Khan Jamali became a national figure as a part of the government of former Prime Minister Nawaz Sharif, and served as the Chief Minister of Balochistan in two non-consecutive terms from June 1988 to December 1988, and 1997 to 1996. 
(4) Although a senior leader in the Pakistan Muslim League (PML) and Sharif's confidante, Jamali's relations went cold, and Zafarullah Khan Jamali subsequently joined the dissident's party after the 1999 coup led by General Pervez Musharraf. 
(5) After participating successfully in the 2002 general election, Jamali won the bid for the Office of Prime Minister after Zafarullah Khan Jamali supporters and colleagues left their respected parties to support him. 
(6) On 21 November 2002, Jamali was appointed as thirteenth Prime Minister of Pakistan, becoming the first ethnic Baloch to hold that office.[5][6] 
 
(7)Jamali vowed to transform Pakistan's economy into broader free-market principles, and implemented intensive economic shock therapy, price liberalization and privatization programmes.[7] 
(8) Zafarullah Khan Jamali political and economic philosophy emphasized the macroeconomics principles and subsequently improved the financial services, revenue and taxation in an attempt to to control foreign debt, hyperinflation and social problems.[7] 
(9) Zafarullah Khan Jamali economic policies reached to expanded record level, producing 13.6% GDP per capita for the national economy.[8] 
(10) Jamali successfully oversaw the country's transformation of two-party system into the multiparty democracy system, and oversaw the implementation of constitutional restoration in the country. 
(11) On 26 June 2004, Jamali announced a surprising announcement of Zafarullah Khan Jamali resignation, leaving the office into hand of Zafarullah Khan Jamali economic minister Shaukat Aziz (although Shuja'at Hussain served as an active Prime minister).[9]
